“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The Inline Weekly-Australia's Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians. —The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank;-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System—one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance,
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility. ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless newspaper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued ,by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct pause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
I my human authority which has not been granted
iy the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
ournal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
iransferred from the columns of a daily paper
ssued from the same office. The Bulletin is
lot connected with any other paper, mid the
natter it contains is all specially prepared by its
iwn unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
bv runners in the streets of every Australasian
lanital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheiler of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maonland
Ranees A paper which is at once the most
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postai offieial- toat,
of all journals issued in Australasia, Ihe Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

In Paris, when a prisoner violently resists
arrest the police get him down and confiscate his
boots, and cut all the buttons off his clothes.
After that he goes quietly. A man whose hands
are both occupied holding on his clothes can’t resist
arrest.
When King William of Prussia — after-
wards first German Emperor—narrowly escaped
death at the hands of Oscar Becker in 1861, the
would-be assassin “was tried on the count for
attempted murder, and, as no life was taken, he
was sentenced to imprisonment with hard labour
for a term of 20 years ” (Lord Augustus Loftus,
Diplomatic Reminiscences.) If the King had
been fortunate enough to be a N.S. W. policeman
under Dibbs, Oscar would have been promptly
hanged. But William being merely a king in a
barbarous, not-right-thinking country, the man
who avowedly tried to kill him was only im-
prisoned.
Dear Bulletin, —There is a man down
here who is commonly known as “ the Hard
Case.” There are many hard cases hereabouts,
but this person is generally recognised as the
hardest. The other day a party of us were out
riding, and just as|we passed a pork and bacon
factory one of the horses shied and landed the
Hard Case senseless on his head on the roadway.
After a serious consultation we resolved that he
was dead, and proceeded to carry his remains
through the factory to the manager’s house at the
back. The motion brought the corpse back to
life, and he looked round and recognised the
place. Then he kicked violently and sat up la
our arms with a shriek: “No, no, NO ! ” he
yelled ; “Oh, my God, no, Bill ! Not sausages!
Bury me.” And with that he sank back again
insensible. —Yours, &c., H.H.8., Sale, Vic.
Dear Bulletin, —“Steele Grey’s ” letter
on the fang formation of Australian snakes is only
another conspicuous and pitiful display of cock-
sure ignorance. As a poet “ Steele Grey ” is,
perhaps, a partial success; as a scientific naturalist
he is a distinct and ludicrous failure. Science is
truth ; poetry is mere imagery. The scientist has
to reason, the poet has only to rhyme. When I
wrote “subsidiary fangs”no one but a hot-headed
sentimental poet would imagine that I meant
teeth. The subsidiary fangs of Australian snakes
don’t come into functional activity—that is they
don’t convey poison into the punctures they make
—until sometime after the first or true fangs have
been broken or removed. Underwood, who
bragged that he had a cure for snakebite, lost his
life through Steele-Grey-like ignorance of the fact
that drawing the first pair of fangs of an Aus-
tralian snake will not render it innocuous. The
black snake (Pseudechis porphyrircus ), for example,
has four subsidiary fangs, so that if two sets of
fangs were drawn there would still be a third
and last pair to come into functional activity
which would be i just as deadly and
dangerous as the first or second pair had been.
I solemnly warn Bulletin readers not to accept
the ipse dixit of the poet “Steele Grey,” in
matters of this kind, but to write (say) to the
Curator of the Australian Museum, Sydney, and
they will find that once more I am right. As
regards “Steele Grey’s ” statement that the so-
called “sting ” of Acanthophis antarctica is not the
last joint of the spine, nothing more need be said.
Eight years ago this very question was decided by
the Australian Museum authorities in my favour.
H. J. M‘Cooky.

Every mother should know the innate nature of her
child, the planet under which it was born, the un-
developed temper, the unformed habits, the particular
trade or calling it is best adapted for, to what sickness,
dictated by the heavenly bodies, it is heir, according
to its nature.
Mme . DE RAYMOND
has been pronounced in Europe and America as the
greatest living wonder of the present age.
If you are sick or in trouble, if you are involved or
unhappy she will make Life Worth Living.

SATURDAY. JULY 7, 1894.
Vok Populi.
Many years ago someone said that “the
voice of the people is the voice of God,”
which vigorous and eloquent-sounding aphor-
ism has done duty even to this day. Yet no
one, in any English-speaking community at
all events, knows what the voice of the
people really is, for it has never had an
opportunity to make itself heard in any
definite or understandable sense. Constitu-
tional Government —which really means
Party Government —only differs from auto-
cracy in degree ; in principle it hardly differs
at all. The one acknowledges that govern-
ment should be the reflex of the popular will,
while the other does not, but the effect of
both systems is to make it as little the
reflex of the will of the governed and
as much a matter of the personal and
private interests of the governors as
possible. In neither case can the people
exercise any direct control over the pro-
cess of law-making ; they can say neither
yea or uay ; they can vote no good measure
and veto no bad one. Under an autocracy
they exact concessions chiefly by threatening
the life of the one law-maker ; under a con-
stitutional system they exact concessions by
threatening the political existence of the
many law-makers; and. under both systems
the result is miserably indecisive. When
the autocrat who will not gcant concessions
is, after much trouble, successfully shot or
dynamited, he is generally sitvjeeded by
another equally uncompromising ; xhen the
Parliament which does not keep pads with
the popular ideas is dissolved, it is genehffly
succeeded by another equally unsatisfactory.
In most cases, neither law-maker knows
what the will of the people really is. It is
not expressed by newspapers, which only
reflect the opinions of their proprietors ; it
cannot be gathered from public meetings,
for comparatively few people attend public
meetings, and a meeting, at the best, only
expresses the opinions of the crowd which
vociferates on the front benches ; neither
can it be ascertained from petitions, for
most people habitually sign petitions without
reading or understanding them. And, above
all, it cannot be learned from Parliamentary
elections,’ which depend mostly on party
management, on the way the votes happen
to be split, on sectarianism, on the
road-and-bridge interest, on the personal
popularity of candidates with the local
Orange lodge, orthepriests, orthe Oddfellows,
or the Buffaloes, or the footballers ; on the
liquor-vote, and on all manner of other con-
tingencies. The popular will can’t be ascer-
tained by any machinery now existing, and
no one knows definitely, or ever has known,
or is likely to know, what it really is.
• # *
When the approaching New South Wales
election is over the local Legislature will
probably be a little further from any accurate
knowledge of this subject than it ever was
before. It is time to abandon the dismal
pretence, which the daily press maintains so
persistently, of professing that the contest
hinges on any definite question whatever It
doesn’t turn upon Protection or Freetrade,
on Federation, Local Government, the State
Bank or the absentee-tax, on land law
reform, on labour legislation or capitalistic
legislation, on republicanism, democracy,
socialism, sectarianism, or any other definite
issue whatever. The result will be arrived
at partly by a hazy mingling of all these and
many other matters ; partly by local influ-
ences ; partly by a vast number of vague and
extraneous influences. In the constituencies
where the strong party runs six candidates
and a weak party runs only one, the weak
party will win, and this arithmetical con-
sideration will do more to decide the result
than anything else. The election in each
one of the 125 electorates will be run on
different issues ; the politician who can fight
a winning battle at Pyrmont must be fear-
fully and wonderfully unlike the politician of
the same party who comes out on top at the
Barwon. When the results are out, and all the
different politicians are added together in the
attempt to make a coherent whole, the pro-
blem will be much the same as that of adding
£6 2s. 6d. to 4 gals. 2qts. and multiplying
the result by a mile and a half or a mile and
three-quarters. Each one will nominally
represent a one-hundred-and-twenty-fifth
part of the alleged will of the people. But
each man will really and truly only know
that he has got in, and which one, or which
47, of his principles or his promises sent
him in, or whether he was sent in because of
any of them, or whether he arrived purely
through the disorganisation of his opponents
and all his principles are loathed and exe-
crated by the great majority of the com-
munity, he will be utterly unable to tell,
Also, the community, which has no definite
means of ascertaining its own mind as a
whole, won’t know either. Even with the
best of intentions to govern according to the
popular will, Parliament is blocked per-
petually by the fact that neither it nor any-
body else knows what that will amounts to.
* # #
The Second Ballot, to which The Bulle-
tin, in common with many others, was once
inclined to pin a considerable amount of
faith, is obviously too feeble an expedient
to suit the case. All it can do is to
provide that, when no one candidate receives
an absolute majority of the votes polled, the
two or three leading men shall be ballotted
for anew, and this only touches the outer
fringe of the difficulty. When ten or twelve
or fifteen candidates are scrambling for a
single seat, as is just now the case in various
electorates, and where three distinct parties
are in existence, and one party is more or
less represented by eight or nine candidates
who all insist on going to the poll, it is quite
possible for the man who, on his own indi-
vidual merits, would command the biggest
vote, to be fourth or fifth on the list, and
to be thus entirely out of the second ballot.
In such an irruption the very weak party,
which has one representative only, may
easily come out on top ; the comparatively
feeble party which runs three representa-
tives may hold the ' second, third and
fourth places ; and the best man of
the strong party which is cursed with
eight candidates may only come in fifth.
Under such conditions the second ballot
would come very little nearer to determining
the will of the people than did the first.
Moreover, the second ballot, and any number
of ballots, even up to the fortieth or more,
would leave the principal evil just where it
was before. They would be so many more
repetitions of the old system of voting for
men instead of measures. They would still
leave the nation in the old position of en-
deavouring to give a coherent verdict on
forty different questions by simply saying
“yes” or “no” to one man’s candidature.
The country would have no more control
over the politician who got in on the second
ballot, if he proved false to his promises,
than over the one who got in according to
the old fashion. It would be one move in
the right direction, but no more ; and, in
many respects, it would leave the hap-hazard
machine of Party Government in almost the
same unsatisfactory condition as at present.
# • •
Among all the political parties in Austra-
lia, there is not one that is honest enough
nor intelligent enough to be worth voting
for on its own merits; the least objectionable
one is only worth supporting in order to
keep out those which are more objectionable.
Even the best of them all does not represent
the voice of the country—unless it does so
unconsciously and by accident. It is on all
these troubles and incoherencies of the exist-
ing system that the demand for the Referen-
dum is based. By that device the country is
able to give a tolerably definite decision on
every important question which comes before
it, and can give it on the instant, whereas the
return of twenty new Parliaments on the
present confused and bewildering plan gives
no definite decision about anything. There
have been three general elections in N.S. W.
since Australian Federation first came
to the front, yet no one is any nearer
knowing the views of the majority on
that subject than at the beginning. There
have been at least ten since Local Govern-
ment became an urgent question, and whether
the country at large really wants a system of
Local Government remains as uncertain as
ever. The present system is not only abso-
lutely independent of public opinion ; it even
leaves it a matter of grave uncertainty
whether there is any definite public opinion
at all.
• # •
The Bulletin urges the abolition of Party
Government and the introduction of the
Referendum simply because the central prin-
ciple of Democracy is that the country should
be ruled by the will of the majority, and it
knows of no other way whereby this can be
effected. It does not advocate the Re-
ferendum merely because it believes
that its own principles would come up-
permost when the will of the com-
munity received free expression, for no
one knows or can know what would
come uppermost under such circumstances.
There is such an absolute want of data to go
upon that it is as profitable to speculate on
the result as it is to guess at the locality of
the lost sepulchre of Moses. But the
experiment would be the greatest and most
interesting event in Australian history, and
it would decide so many vexed questions,
and abolish such vast quantities of useless poli-
tics, that, in whatever fashion it resulted, it
would be a blessed relief to the community.
And, however it turned out, it would at least
make one thing certain : the country would
thereafter get exactly the kind of Govern-
ment it deserved —no better and no worse.
The Social Bearings of Her Majesty.
When the Duchess of York fulfilled her royal
duty by giving birth to an heir, the Argus was
delivered of another sort of little leading article
which, to Tory eyes, would seem calm and
beautiful. The day for hysteric jubilation at
the advent of a prince has happily passed away
never to return. Unadulterated drivel on such
matters is no longer acceptable even to an
AKGUS-reading crowd, and in its place we get a
specious apology for the Throne, worded with
some respect for the advance of popular educa-
tion. The Tory press does not keep pace with
the times, but, recognising that the times are
getting further ahead than ever, it makes a
pathetic attempt to stagger after them. For-
merly the Crown’s idolaters refused to discuss
the fetish’s infallibility. To admit the existence
of a doubt was to blaspheme. Nowadays they
plead their cause witli the ingenuity of a bar-
rister briefed for the defence, saying everything
passible in extenuation of the sin, and manu-
facturing much of the dirt that they fling at the
other side. Thus the Melbourne organ :
There may be some people in England and among the
great communities over which the English flag waves who
dream of establishing a republic, and who delude them-
selves into the obvious and absurd error that something
might be gained by attacking the monarchy. But what
good could possibly be accomplished? As the constitu-
tion is now administered, the Crown has no right of veto
compared with that which is exercised by the President
of the United States. All the freedom that can be con-
sidered either desirable or necessary for the British people
is already secured, or is within their grasp. They are not
hampered by the corruption of American politics or the
peculiar weakness of the French system of government.
They have in the monarchy an institution that is not
adverse to political progress and that is most powerful in
society ; they have as a Sovereign one who can stoop to no
political intrigue, who can consent to no log-rolling, who
is altogether above the ordinary sordid and selfish and
mean considerations of political partisanship. There is
no earthly reason why the monarch should be deposed in
order to make room for the creature of an election, who
is bound to favour the party that assisted him with its
votes and its money.
Unnecessary to reprint a further three inches of
perennial remarks concerning the “ personal
attachment of English people to the Royal
Family,” and the public interest felt in the
“domestic events” of the Family aforesaid.
Nobody can read newspapers without learning
that a Duke of York marries a wife, and that
the wife has a baby in due course. Public
interest is forced upon the community, and the
maternal instincts of womankind keep it alive.
To the ladies— God bless ’em !—anybody’s
marriage, or the first production of anybody’s
baby, will always be important matters when
they have any kind of acquaintance with the
happy pair. Their acquaintance with this
particular pair is mainly pictorial. If Mrs.
Jones had a baby on the same day as the
Duchess, the “domestic event” of Mrs. J.
interested her friends far more than did the
arrival of the Yorkling. It was a mere
question of knowing Mrs. Jones. As for men
in general, they could be as easily entertained
by a florid description of Jem Corbett’s
nuptials as by the particulars of the grandest
royal wedding on earth. Also, if Mrs. Cor-
bett’s baby were advised, by cable, six months
in advance, and if the cable made much of the
fact that Corbett’s mother-in-law intended to
be there at the time, then the actual coming of
the bruiser’s infant would stir the great heart
of the people to some extent. Anyway, the
alleged personal attachment of English people
to “ royal eventors ” is not much argument for
the Throne. They are attached to plum-pud-
ding, and gin, and heaps of other reprehensible
functions.
The Tory defence of monarchy is careful not
to touch upon that hideous social tumour which
has its roots deep down in the royal system.
True enough that the outward political appear-
ance of the Queen is healthy. She serves a
certain purpose very well, and if she stopped
at that point the elderly lady would not matter
much. Regarded as a perpetual President —
confirming the acts of a Gladstone or a Salis-
bury with equal promptitude in both cases—
the Queen is objectionable only on the score of
expense. It is not, however, the immediate
cost of monarchy, but the results of class dis-
tinction, springing from monarchy, that work
incalculable harm to mankind. Indeed, if the
Queen lived in a White House, and her royalty
didn’t exceed the social pomp of an American
President, and were she not a fount of titular
“honour” and the cause of K.C.M.G.’s, demo-
cracy might be for ever content to keep the
Government figurehead in one fam i ly. Theblessed
Regalia, and the custom of walking backwards
from the Presence, and all the other pernicious
Court mummeries, would disappear, and it
would be absolutely necessary to restrict the
Sovereign to a modest income that there could
be no fear of the reigning family amassing great
wealth—but with these alterations a monarchy
might perhaps exist in the enlightened future.
The name of King or Queen would lose its
tinsel glory, and become merely a plain symbol!
of a straightforward system of Government,,
whilst the children of the hereditary president,,
no longer known as Royal Highnesses, wouldl
possess little charm for the illustrated weeklies,,
and incite no grovel.
# * *
A monarchy that forms the centre of widen-
ing circles of class distinction is a poisonous-
thing. Because the institution is ‘ ‘ most powerful
in society,” society needs reforming altogether.
Royal majesty must be hedged round by heredi-
tary nobility, and “ nobility ” of this kind is the
base end of bloated brewers and selfish capi-
talists in every department of money-making
business. The poor grow poorer that the rich
may become rich enough to get into “the
Prince’s set.” Workmen are pinched, kindly
impulses are strangled, the bright ambition of
manly industry dies with the attainment of
wealth, and the fruits of success are cast before
swine as the result of monarchial influence upon
society. With the abolition of royal glamour
the chief inducement to snobbery, which is the
germ of so-called “loyalty,” would be abol-
ished. How much suffering and degradation
does humanity owe to its own inherent selfish-
ness, and what is the Royal Family but a Royal
Society for the Encouragement of Mean Ideals,
and the Promotion of Pitiful Ambition ?
The America that Washington dreamed of
might have come to pass save for those
European circles of social distinction into which
the almighty dollar is a passport. Yankee
plutocrats trample upon the banner of frater-
nity in their anxiety to make the acquaintance
of a visiting earl, and a Mackay piles
gold that his wife may buy a disreputable
prince for her girl borne of her own
body. A monarch of the existing type creates
baneful uses for money. Men who start in life
with good intentions as to the employment of
the capital they toil for, are ultimately seduced
into the ranks of title-seekers and tuft-hunters.
The evil can be traced to the monarchial system
as surely as that bad system is traceable to the
teachings of a priesthood which entitles God the
“ King of Kings.”
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Reign of Brummagem.
Dear Bulletin, —Your correspondent, “Corn-
stalk,” argues from the patent fact that the
foreign-horn grab most of the Australian poli-
tical billets, that they are more enterprising
than the Australians. His inference is wrong.
True it is the Tommy Dodds, Holmans,
Bradleys, Rosas, make most noise in the poli-
tical field—not because they have more push
than the native-born, but from the fact that
they belong to the numericaily-stronger part of
the population. It is not a question of political
ethics but of simple proportion. I have gone
through the census-reports of all the provinces
and find that out of 1,090,000 men of legal age
only 405,000 are Australian-born ! Put the lest
of age up to 30 years, and of 734,000 not more
than 203,000 were born in this country ! The
Australians over 30 years are 40,000 less
numerous than the English, and very little
more than twice the Irish ! For the seven pro-
vinces the figures are :
As the bulk of the politicians are men over 30,
the Australians are scarcely entitled to one
place in four, and that is just about the propor-
tion of phices they contrive to secure. There is
not one province in which the Australians of
adult age come to more than three-fourths of
the foreign-born, while in Queensland they are
less, and in Maoriland scarcely more than one-
fourth ! If the people who make the sentiment
of the country are alone considered —those over
30 years of age —there is no province that can
boast more than sixty Australians to every
hundred of the imported article, while in
Maoriland the proportion is less than twelve,
and in Queensland less than five to the
hundred.
These figures offer all the explanation that is
needed of Patterson’s frothy invitation to May
and George, the twenty-one guns in honour of
the birth of a princeling, Suttor’s journey of
10,000 miles to move a resolution congratulating
Queen Victoria on the commencement of her 57th


year of office, the almost unanimous passage of
the Naval Tribute Bill through the Parliaments,
and many a like proceeding. Not every man
born in Australia loves las country, and there
are but few British or foreign-born whose Aus-
tralian sentiments will stand much wear and
tear. Protestations do not count tor much,
else LiLLEY would not have accepted a title nor
Seddon pined for one. The majority do not
speak of “Home” without meaning to endorse
the sentiment that the term implies—they are
sojourners in a strange land. No man ever
lived who had the feelings of a patriot towards
any country except the one in which he was
born and nurtured. Resentment against one s
mother-land is a common enough fading, but
patriotism towards an adopted country is made
of different stuff. What imported patriot
would not whoop as loudly for Paraguay as he
does to-day for Australia if the cons.deralion
received warrantee} the change ! 1 he man who
looks for true Australian sentiment from othei
than Australian born, indulges a vain hope
My arithmetic should show him he need not
expect such sentiment to be matured until the
native-born predominate, which will not >e
until more than 20 years are counted out.—
Theodore Woinakski.
Prohibition Goes Under.
The people of the prohibition settlement of
Mildura, Vic., have decided, by a solid majority,
that they want their dry and desolate town put
on the same footing—in the matter of liquor—
as the rest of the province. Prohibition has
had a fair trial in Mildura, and has panned out
miserably. There are more drink-shops in it
than in any other town of its size, and also
more drink, and the drink is more expensive
and more execrable than anywhere else in Vic-
toria. The police cannot put it down, except
by arresting practically the whole population ;
they are in the position of a small boy em-
ployed to keep the flies off a decayed carcase,
and as they can’t chase all the flies at once it
follows that, even in their times of wildest
activity, anyone cafi get raving drunk at Mil-
dura at the shortest notice. The Mildura drink-
sellers are difficult to convict, for the popular
sympathies are with them —that the populace
should sympathise with the vendors of such
awfulliquorsfls remarkable, but presumably they
do it because it is the best drink obtainable.
The local grog-dealers run their trade day and
night, Sundays and week-days; and as they
have to make up the amount of their fines as
well as ordinary trade profits they charge
exorbitant prices and- sell their compounds in
unclean cellars and dens to avoid observation.
Even the prohibitionists who go to Mildura
often grow disgusted with their own creed,
which they find to be the drunkest creed ever
invented. As might be expected, however, the
popular decision that Mildura wants to run in
future on ordinary local-option principles has
roused the Victorian teetotal party to fury.
Wherever liquor is legalised, that party demands
that the local majority shall have power to
decide whether it shall be sold or not; but in
Mildura it isn’t legalised, and the proposal that
the majority there shall have the same power
as the majority everywhere else has caused
every Cold Tea fanatic in the land to rear up
and protest. The teetotal creed is simply that
the majority should rule where Cold Tea is in
the majority, and that the minority should rule
where Cold Tea is in the minority. Which
short and simple Confession of Faith only
amounts to the decision that Cold Tea should
prevail at all times and under all circum-
stances
The “ Depression.”
Now that the New South Wales elections are at
hand the “ prevailing depression ” is being run
by the Foreign-trade press for all it is worth—
which isn’t very much. Almost every article
of common consumption is cheaper now in New
South Wales under the blighting influences of
partial Protection, than it was three or four
years ago under the “ Freetrade ” regime ; and
as the shoddy press has always circulated the
industrious lie that Protection makes all things
dear instead of cheap, 1 this result is set down in
a hazy fashion to the same old depression.
There is nothing else that it can be set down to
which won’t utterly give away the Freetrade
superstition, so the,depression is dragged in as
a matter of necessity. But when the fall of
prices is cousideretl from the producer’s stand-
point—when the same papers are talking about
the situation of the seller instead of that of the
buyer —the result is put down with a dull thud
to the horrors of Protection in its worst shape.
This method of looking at the same fact from
two different sides produces a curious state of
logic. When an article which used to fetch Bd.
in the good old days is now sold for fid., the
fact that the buyer has only to pay fid. is owing
to the depression, while the other fact that the
seller only receives fid. is another convincing
evidence of the cussedness of the present tariff
The obvious fact, which is entirely ignored, is
that the depression is a very old institution in
the land, and that it was in full blast in
the shoddy days just as it is now. More-
over, when the Foreign-trade press isn’t
lamenting about how Protection has made
prices low for the seller, and explaining
about how depression has made them low for
the buyer, it generally devotes itself to publish-
ing theoretical essays about the awful manner
in which Protection enhances prices, and the
way it lays ponderous burdens on the backs of
the poor, and taxes the masses for the benefit of
a handful of bloated monopolists. When it is
in this mood it flatly denies the reduction in
prices altogether, and only curses the depression
as a side-issue which has arisen somehow
through the Dibbs tariff. Altogether Foreign-
trade is in an even more confused state than
usual just now, and the old spinal curvature of
its intelligence has assumed an aggravated
form.
Hume Nisbet’s Romancing 1 .
The coming Australian novelist will have, when
he arrives, a rather hard job cut out for him. He
will have to get the English reader to unlearn
almost everything that former novelists have
taught him about the conditions of life on our
continent. To judge from the class of books
accepted as typical of Australia the average reader
must suppose life in the bush to be a long series
of hand to hand conflicts with remarkably
ferocious bushrangers, combined with much drink-
ing, shooting of aboriginals, and shouting of coo-ee.
It was an evil day for us when Hume Nisbet for-
sook painting for novel writing. His latest insult
to this country, called “ A Bush Girl’s Romance,
is simply a mass of childish nonsense not even
redeemed by decent writing, and yet it is gravely
asserted in reviews that it bears the stamp of
truth. Nay, more, the author has the brazen
front to assert in his preface that his awful out-
pouring is “ a true picture of Australia in its
western portion.” In the beginning we make the
acquaintance of the familiar traveller who is
approaching a salt lake, and who thus soliloquises :
“ Ten days since I have tasted anything except
gum leaves. Rive days since I had my last taste
of water, and on my back I carry a thou-
sand pounds in nuggets.” After such an
unheard - of spell of endurance he naturally
falls senseless and is rescued by _ three
horsemen who come up at “ a swinging
gallop”—of course, every one always rides at a
gallop in the Australian bush. These horsemen
are well-dressed in fashionable clothes, and, natur-
ally, they are bushranghrs. This is simply a
sample of the wild absurdities crammed into the
volume. Farther on Mr. Nisbet grows serious
and states that—“ln Australia, where the Govern-
ment murder men, women, and infants, as the
Sepoys are said to have murdered the wives and
children of our soldiers, it is called by the mild
and inoffensive term of dispersing.” After this
sweeping charge he goes on to say that —
“ The aboriginal is trapped, poisoned, shot, bayo-
netted, and otherwise destroyed, as energetically
as they are trying to destroy those other pests, the
rabbits.” Now, considering that in. the two
rabbit-infested provinces of N.S. Wales and Vic-
toria it would be impossible to find a myall native
to poison or bayonet, Mr. Hume Nisbet is certainly
a first-class Munchausen on the face of it. Here
is another little gem, anent the treatment of black
gins : “ As half-caste children are reckoned to be
inconvenient and dangerous as evidence of im-
morality, which Government has to punish when
discovered . . . the savage mothers are de-
puted to destroy them.” Naturally, proba-
bility is scouted throughout the work. Gold
is picked up in nuggets scattered loosely
around on the surface; the blacks poison
their spears with the venom of the “ deaf-adder, ”
&c., &c. And this puerile trash, worse than the
extravagances of a penny novel of the “Deadwood
Dick ” series, is received in all faith as a true
picture of Australia. We have partly ourselves
to blame for it. Our own writers of fiction have
a sneaking affection for the convict and the bush-
ranger, and did not some warlike “team” or
other give a graphic display in London of a
combat between robbers and the military, alleged
to be so common here? Really it seems that
when our novelist does put in an appearance he
will be received with discredit as a man who
knows nothing about Australia, unless he wades
in gore and brains all through three volumes.
A parson has painted a fancy picture of
“ Christ in Melbourne,” repudiating the idea
that He would “walk up and down Bourke-
street in the unpicturesque garb and talking
the everyday language of a modern artisan.”
“ Suppose Christ were to come to Melbourne ! ”
says this clerical visionary. Well, The Bul-
letin will suppose the result:—
At a meeting held in Sydney last Friday to
consider the famous Costa Rica Packet case,
Mr. “Jack” Want, Q.C., took occasion to
point out the base ingratitude of Britain in
this matter, considering how N.S. Wales came
to her assistance with the famous Soudan Con-
tingent some years ago. This is the sort of
bunkum which makes any calm and collected
Cornstalk desire to go out and hide himself up
a tree in the far wilderness till the awful
Soudan fiasco is entirely forgotten—if it ever
will be forgotten. Only the most brassy
kind of barrister would dig up that lament-
able episode, nowadays, and describe it
seriously as “coming to the assistance” of
Britain. The only thing that is certain about
the contingent, barring the bill, is that Britain
didn’t know what on earth to do with it when
it came, and was very much embarrassed by
its presence all through. Also, there is every
reason to believe that the beloved mother
country was very glad indeed to see the last of
it when it went away, and vowed most
solemnly never to have any more contingents of
the kind if it could possibly be avoided. The
polite, though stern, refusal given to the offers
of various other contingents from adjacent
provinces, also suggests that Britain awoke to
an understanding of the situation at a very
early date. Probably the recollection of that
ghastly event has something to do with the
mother-country’s present unsatisfactory attitude
in the Costa Rica Packet case. Hingland, it
may be, has been brooding over the Contingent
episode ever since, and now takes the first
opportunity to get level with New South Wales
for that irruption. Humour isn’t the average
Briton’s strong point, and the idea of sending a
kind of PuNCH-and-JuDY show-including field-
artillery horsed with ’bus mokes that wouldn t
stir a yard until they heard a noise like unto that
of a ’bus door slamming—to his assistance at a
time when he had plenty of stern realities in
hand, may have seemed to him rather a ill-
timed pleasantry.

tame variety, was fined <£‘2o last week in the
Water Police-court, and two other persons
were fined <£s each, for distributing a pamphlet
called “ A Handbook of Anarchy without an
imprint. The alternative in each case was
three months imprisonment, and as Andrews
and his friends had no money they were sent to

an imprint on the pamphlet, though not a
printer’s imprint, to wdt : “ Issued by J. A.
Andrews, late of Mudgee, and now 7 of 491
Elizabeth-street, Sydney.” The spirit of the
law, which merely seeks to fix the responsi-
bility of issue, was thus complied with, and as
the defendants most likely sinned against its
letter in ignorance of the breach of technicality
the punishment was vindictive and cruel.
There can be no doubt that the prosecution
was influenced by -the character of the
views supposed to be put forward by Andrews.
Had his pamphlet been a fulsome congratula-
tion of York’s wife for having added another
royalty to the burdens of the British taxpayer,
there would have been no prosecution what-
ever. Andrews’ views w 7 ere moderate and in-
telligent, if not very novel, and he expressly
points out in his book that there is nothing to
support the common delusion that anarchy is
synonymous with a policy of violence. To
degrade the Government by such cow'ardly
exhibitions of official malevolence is the surest
way to create anarchy in Australia.
A meeting of portly citizens w 7 as held last week
in Sydney to consider the political situation
and to urge the community to vote for “good
men ” only at the forthcoming general election.
The meeting also passed a resolution that what
the country wanted was not so much politics or
parties or political principles as a strong deter-
mination to return only the “ very best men.”
The crowd which passed this resolution con-
sisted largely of wool-brokers, merchants,
lawyers, money-lenders (Mr. Rogalsky was
much in evidence), financiers, brewers, account-
ants, importers, spirit-dealers, stock-and-
station agents, auctioneers, softgoodsmen,
and others of the kind, and these
gentlemen evidently regard themselves as fair
samples of the kind’of men who were wanted if
the bleeding country is to be saved. There were
many sleek, elderly men among them ; and a
few unhealthy-looking, flaccid persons with
those little bags of skin under their eyes which
suggest kidney-disease and a tendency to break
up suddenly and unexpectedly ; and a number
with large, dough-coloured double chins. There
was hardly one in the crowd who has ever been
heard of as doing anything specially good for
the benefit of anybody, but they were obviously
solid on the assumption that they were the
“ very best men,” and formed an interesting
mutual admiration society on the strength of
it. Probably enough they were right in their
assumption after all. For the good man, as
the term is understood in financial circles, is
not the man who has ever done any good, or
who has ever advocated anything good ; it is
the copious, affluent, well-dressed individual
whose feet have a solid grip upon the earth,
and who has a deep, port-wine laugh. And the
verv best men have all these symptoms, and
are blessed in addition with stomachs of great
magnitude, on account of which the} 7 haven’t
seen their own feet for the best part of a
generation.
The harassing block of New South Wales
Supreme Court law-suits compels public atten-
tion once again to the law-“ vacation ” ques-
tion. On the three lists for the term just
concluded there were 199 causes set down for
hearing. The Judges have now gone into mid-
winter vacation, and more than half of those
causes are, with machine-like frigidity, left
over to the September term. This may spell
ruin to many of the litigants to whom delay
means inability to continue their usual busi-
nesses ; or it may only mean “short commons,’
or the dropping of, perhaps, righteous claims.
Even to the man with a well-lilled purse, it
means extra expense. Only the lawyers benefit
by it. In an abstract consideration of the pro-
tecting potency of justice, the law, in a free
country, should be approachable all the year
round. If, as matters now 7 stand, the vacations
in vogue are absolutely necessary to the
periodical recuperation of the Judges, then
additional acting-Judges should be commis-
sioned to hear the cause-list through to the
very last case. It is scandalous that over 100
Supreme Court cases should be shelved for tw r o
months in order that their Honors may take a
three-weeks’ holiday.
The compulsory residents of Pentridge stockade
show a growing disposition to “bash” warders,
and the Yic.~ Solicitor-General proposes to
abolish this scandal with a new Bill. The new
Bill is to be a brief, free-and-easy measure,
enabling prisoners to get flogged at a minute’s
notice, more or less, for an “unprovoked
assault ” upon a warder. There is no doubt in
the mind of the Government as to the gratuitous
villainy of these prison outbreaks. Patterson
and his lamblike brethren have never taken the
trouble to notice that Pentridge “ discipline,”
which dates back to the days of John Price,
is in itself a provocation to bang the first uni-
formed myrmidon who comes near. Lawanorder
has no eyes for flaws in its precious penal system.
The duty of society is to keep out of Pentridge,
and, if a section of society gets in, it must take
Pentridge as it finds that place, and love the
warders, and be always good. Patterson and
Co. would support a Bill for walloping sinners
who committed assaults upon Beelzebub when
they were “doing their little bit” in hell,
without any consideration to the possible defects
of hell as a school of repentance.
If Christ to Melbourne shortly came,
A bearer of salvation,
Some “leading men ” (whom we could name)
Would meet Him at the station,
Whilst soldiers warded off the crowd,
And “ bobby ” waved his truncheon,
The boomers, singing praises loud.
Would take their Lord to luncheon.
Would drive Him off to Mister ’May a.
And welcome Him with speeches,
They’d chaperon Him everywhere,
They’d stick to Him like leeches.
With upraised eye, and accent bland,
And Sunday best behaviour,
That puffy, pawky chosen baud
Would glorify the Saviour.
When “ confidence” was quite restored
By these religious-sectors,
They’d start a bank and swear the Lord
Was chairman of directors;
And, as the cash deposits swelled,
They’d use the old devices
For working off the land they held
At most abnormal prices.
Ah ! if He but to Melbourne came!
No Jews would now despise Him ;
The Fat Men of financial fame
Would boldly advertise Him,
And, when He left them to their fraud,
They’d rise at greasy dinners,
To bless their dear departed Lord,
Who came to ruin sinners.

The Wild Oat Column.
The B.N.Z. reconstructed last week, for the third
time within seven years. In 1888 this institution wrote
Off £300,000 of capital and about £600,000 of reserve fund
in one fatal act, to cover losses, and having thus wiped off
the accumulated results of years of reckless trading in the
boom period, It started with a clean sheet, and called up
more capital. Right up to the time of this disaster the
balance-sheets represented it as a solvent and flourishing
Concern, and the loss of £900,000 was a woeful disillusion-
ment. Next year another thunderbolt fell from a clear
•ky, and £BOO,OOO more capital was written off, the alleged
oleanlng-up of 1888 having been as unreliable as the pre-
vious state of alleged prosperity. After that the B.N.Z.
went on steadily. It was everywhere believed that the
revelations of 1889 included all the bad debts and disasters
of the old rotten days ; the institution paid a humble 5 per
cent. div. regularly, and began to gather up a new reserve
in a small way ; it issued £500,000 more capital, and the
old shareholders buried their dead cash and made the best
of the situation.
Last week, however, ib was suddenly announced
in the Maoriland Parliament that the B.N.Z. was once
more in a desperate situation, and apparently a fresh
batch of “revelations” is at hand. The situation was so
awful that a bill was rushed through the House in one
sitting, to provide lor the issue of £2,000,000 worth of
preference shares, said shares, and a 4 per cent, dividend
thereon, being guaranteed by the State. Half-a-million
is also to be called up from the present unlucky share-
holders, a circumstance which, to many of them, must
mean utter ruin. The head office, which was shifted to
London at the time of the last mishap, is to be removed
to Wellington; the accounts are to be constantly
inspected by Government auditors, the management is to
be subject to the direction of the Treasurer, and the
chairman of directors is to be appointed by the Executive
Council. The new preference capital is nominally repay-
able at par in 10 years, and in the event of default the
Government will appoint a receiver, which will be practi-
cally equivalent to taking over the B.N.Z. and making it
a State institution.
It would probably have been more satisfactory
if the State had taken over the bank bodily on the spot.
The troubles of that lamentable concern seem endless,
and, though it is doubtless good policy to avert the crash
which was imminent, the device of guaranteeing any
private institution, no matter under what limitations, is
a preordained failure. The B.N.Z. is not an affair of the
first magnitude, yet in seven years it has written off
£1,200,000 of the shareholders’ money as absolutely lost.
It has issued £500,000 of new capital, it proposes now to
call up £500,000 more, and it still requires £2,000,000 under
a State guarantee to keep it afloat. To all intents and
purposes, therefore, it seems to have got into difficulties
to the extent of £4,000,000, which ghastly record
is hardly calculated to inspire confidence in the future.
Considering the humble 5 per cent, which it has paid on
its present capital of £900,000, the prospects of ordinary
shareholders for the next few years, after the dividend of
4 per cent, on £2,000,000 of preference capital is pro-
vided for, seem to be about nil. The impending call will
be a melancholy experience for many shareholders.
Maoriland had a childlike faith in its big bank in former
years, and the fortunes of many widows and orphans was
invested in B.N.Z. shares in the days when that institu-
tion was founded on a rock. There will be very little
left of these fortunes when the third reconstruction is
completed.
During the former troubles the B.N.Z. shifted
its headquarters to London, with that humble faith which
the “ kawlinist ” has always manifested in the capacity of
the British financier. The result shows that the British
financier is very ordinary clay, after all.
The fatality which has followed the B.N.Z is
another warning to that dying father who leaves his
family a small fortune invested in hank shares, and then
passes away with a blissful consciousness that their
future is provided for. Australia is a very new country,
yet it can probably show already as many dead banks as
it can banks that are yet alive. It will probably show a
good many more dead banks before the close of the
century. The’visible moral is that every bank, like every
human being, dies sooner or later, and the best than can
be hoped is that it will go later. Generally, however, it
goes sooner. How many unquiet ghosts of dead husbands
and fathers must roam around the sites of the old Bank
of Australia, the three defunct banks of S.A., the two
Meroantiles, the Federal, the Bank of Van Diemen’s Land,
the Provincial and Suburban, the forgotten Australian
and European, the twin Orientals, the Bank of Queens-
land, the Bank of Sydney and the old-time Bank of Port
Phillip. It is less than 80 years since the first Australian
bank was founded—and what an awful record 1
• * * *
According to the cabled report, the B.N.Z.
officials only realised last week the straits to which their
office was reduced. Judging by the state of the share-
market, however, it would appear as if some person had
realised the situation, or a portion of it, a good many
months ago, and judiciously rushed his shares on the
market accordingly. Who was that person, and where
did he get his information ?
• • • »
The balance-sheet of the reconstructed London
Bank of Australia is a depressing document for the
ordinary shareholder, and none too cheering for the pre-
ference animal. During the five months ended 31st Dec.
it earned a net profit of barely £6OOO, out of which it pays
a dividend of 5J per cent, to preference shareholders, and
carries £2OOO forward. As the preference shareholders
represent only one-sixth of the capital, the chances of the
ordinary shareholders getting anything for some years to
come seem infinitesimal. The appalling shrinkage is attri-
buted, as usual, to the reconstruction troubles, hut as the
interest on that overwhelming item, “ bills discounted,
loans and other securities in London and the colonies,
£6,350,000,” booms along serenely in wet weather and dry,
the explanation is difficult of comprehension. If these
six millions of investments really did yield the alleged
profits of last year it would be interesting to know what
blight has fallen on them since. And if they didn’t, why
was it alleged that they did? This same conundrum
glares forth from the balance-sheets of all the recon-
structed banks, with about two exceptions, and the only
coherent theory is that the profits of past days consisted
largely of irrecoverable interest charged against the over-
drafts of more or less stony customers. Which over-
drafts, by the way, generally figure as assets to this day,
and form part of that historic item, “ bills discounted and
all other securities, so many millions.”
• * • *
The Abgus suggests that the depositors in re-
constructed banks are receiving 4J per cent, interest “at
the expense of the community,” and it argues that “ the
present dispensation cannot last." The obvious corollary
is to repudiate the agreement to pay 4J and renew at 3
per cent, for so much of eternity as the banks are likely
to last. Now, it Hancock had talked this way, we’d have
laughed, but to listen to such a mastodon specimen of
contract-breaking from the only Argus makes the tears
sizzle down one’s nose.

mortgage is so tainted. We disbelieve the canard, which
must have been feathered and Winged by someone looking

Matt. Larkin, the jolly embezzler of South Mel-
bourne, has once more been served in Pentridge prison
with criminal summonses, charging him with further
separate embezzlements amounting in all to £5389. Con-
trast this steady pursuit of a caged convict, whose
friends have evidently deserted him in his adversity,
after helping to squander stolen moneys, and the enthusi-
astic and almost reverential adoration of even bigger male-
factors who have got off scot free, by potent influences
that won’t bear scrutiny.

N.S.W. Insolvency-Registrar Henry indicated
by his remarks when suspending a bankrupt’s certificate
last week, that he considers a man who trades on money
borrowed at 15 per cent, has no reasonable or probable
expectation of being able to pay his debts. It may be
impossible to fix a rule in such matters, but the opinion
is suggestive.
* * * *
The very latest in insurance. You apply to
half-a-dozen offices for work as a canvasser, giving as
reference a well-known M.L.A. His reference is honoured,
and in several cases you get the appointment. Then you
write, stating that you are just starting to round-up the
residents, and will the office be so kind as to send you a
hand-bag to carry your documents in? Some of them are
delighted to do it. Having secured six hand-bags on the
cheap, you then start to rake in fountain-pens, type-
writers, and other things on the same system. All goes
well until two managers happen to meet at lunch, and
start to compare notes about you.
• * * *
Dear Bulletin, —What’s a good cure for wool-
canvassers? The pest is very bad this season. Sixteen
altogether have been at my place the last two months.
Just to spite ’em, I’m going to ship.—Central Full Area,
N.S.W.

Dear Bulletin, —150 acres of land, 100 miles
from Melbourne —capacity, 1 rabbit to 5 acres—my valua-
tion, 7s. 6d. per acre. Hank advanced £4 10s. an acre.
Crash—reconstruction. Land figures in bank balance-
sheet, with three years’ interest at 9 per cent, added, £5
14s. per acre. It true value were put for false in all those
reconstructed balance-sheets, how long would the unsound
banks stand? —Pisces.
* • * «
Labourers on private building contracts in
Sydney are now working for 4Jd. an hour, because con-
tracts are taken by men of straw for next to nothing. On
receiving the first advance in coin for work accomplished,
it frequently happens that the snide contractor simply
vanishes without remembering to pay his wages men. A
builder who called recently for tenders for the erection of
a small brick cottage received 108 applications. The
tenders for brickwork ranged from £l4 to £46 ; those
for the carpentering, from £l6 to £6O. The differences
between these minima and maxima convey some idea of
the gulf in financial calibre that lies between the man
who does and him who does not pay his workmen.

Scene, Hobart a week or two ago. Present,
mining-expert and Mount Hoaxley shareholder. Latter
demands, as old friend, expert’s private opinion re the
“golden Mount.” Expert replies: “I am under agree-
ment to divulge nothing until I meet the syndicate in
Barataria.” “Whisper it in French," persistently
demanded the shareholder—“Bon ou mal?" “Bon,"
replies the expert. Ye gods!

The men charged with salting Mt. Hoaxley
have been discharged at Hobart, the Govt, being unable
to establish a prima facie case. This is the last scene of
the farce. The mountain is solitary ; the Ten Tribe* have
been dispersed anew; and the manager of Mt. Morgan,
sitting serenely in his back-yard, smiles as the incident is
engulfed in the enormous maw of the Angel of Oblivion.

A man who, a few weeks ago, was so desperately
anxious to be “in ” Mount Huxley that he paid £7O for a
£25 share, now writes to a Hobart sharebroker seeking to
recover £45 from the vendor. “No conscientious man,”
he writes, “ would stain his soul by retaining money thus
acquired.” His request opens up a wide field of conjec-
ture as to what his own action would have been had
Huxley realised Mr. Samuel’s fond expectations, and the
shares gone up “ kite high.”

The second crushing at the Wyalong battery,
Foster and party’s, gave 1420 z. from 43 tons—surface
stone. Tub Bulletin is waiting to hear of solid reefs and
deep level crushings before flinging up its hat. There
is talk of starting a co-operative battery, to include a
chlorinating plant. Wate’r for drinking is ss. per cask.
* * * *
The new alluvial rush at Garangula (N.S.W.)
reports 50 claims on gold. The ground belongs to a
squatter who charges Is. a ton for carting, Is. for sluicing,
and 2s. Od. royalty, and hands you the difference after
banking the stuff, so that one man is making damper and
mutton.
* » • *
Systematic ore-stealing at the Lord Jersey mine,
near Tenterfield (N.S.W.), has resulted in the discharge of
all hands employed. The mine is isolated and surrounded
by scrub, so that, with accomplices in the neighbouring
township of Drake, theft and concealment were easy.

For six months, ended June 30, Bendigo mines
yielded 78,0000 z. gold, and paid in divs. £76,800, against
balls £7o,3oo—not a very liberal surplus. Both yield and
divs. are less than last year’s; Johnson’s topped the list;
paying diva. £11,900; Garden Gully came next with
£8378.-
« • • •
The disgraceful thing about the new find at
Coolgardie—“a second Bayley’a”—is that the discoverers
are all “other aiders." Mills is a N.S.W. station-hand,
Carter, Gardiner, and Dawson also come from N.S.W.,
and Huxley and Elliott from Victoria. Conservative
Westralian disgust is immeasurable. Almost as bad is the
fact that the party are nearly all new-chum miners.
Elliott is an old hand, and Dawson has handled a pick
before, but all the rest are amateurs. That such men
should drop across “the grandest patch of gold yet un-
earthed in the world,” a mile from a main road where
thousands of diggers have passed, ana only 10 or 12 miles
from Coolgardie township, is merely another illustration
of the truism that luck is everything in gold-mining.
The description of the treasure is pretty tall;
“The gold was as thick and as big as plums in a rich
Christmas pudding, and where the gold plums ended the
gold currants commenced. In some of the stone there
appears to be a band of solid gold.” When ‘Big Ben’
was hauled out one or two of the strongest strained them-
selves to lift him. He weighs over 3001 b., and has
several ribs of gold, and his internal arrangements seem
to consist very largely of that metal. Big Ben has been
considered good enough by Elliot to purchase for £2OOO,
and some say Elliott will make over £lOOO on the deal.
Besides Big Ben were quite a number of little Bens,
several containing over lOOoz. each."
“ The show is better than anything ever seen in
Bayley’s Reward. The ironstone, with which reef gold is
usually associated in the district, is entirely absent. The
gold is carried in clear quartz, slightly amber-tinted.
The mine is marvellously rich in coarse, wiry gold, of a
bright colour, the metal being so thickly scattered
through the stone that after the rock is fractured it has
to be wrenched apart. The stone is close-grained. Every
piece broken shows the faces of the fracture to consist of
nearly one-third gold. The excavation, from which fully
£25,000 in dollied gold and specimens have already been
obtained, is not sufficiently large for bringing in a Shet-
land pony. The rich shoot stood up 3ft. above the sur-
face. When Mills found it it required no prospecting to
discover it. Mills said he could see the gold 20ft. away
sticking out of the solid white quartz as well as the noon-
day sun. He hung his hat on one of the gold junks.
The find was kept dark from May 8 till June 26
—seven weeks—while the men, armed, slept over their El
Dorado every night. At last the secret leaked out. The
jumping brigade began operations at once by collaring
two outside leases of four and five acres which the party
had taken up. They even tried to upset the central lease
of 25 acres, but the warden refused to receive the com-
plaint. “The curse of Coolgardie is jumping," says
Mills, the finder of the Londonderry. Mills only went to
Coolgardie in March, so he hasn’t been long in making bis
fortune. The others were scarcely older residents.
Already two of the party have sold their inter-
ests. Henry Dawson, an elderly man, whose family reside
at Summer Hill, Sydney, has transferred to Huxley and
Mills for £3ooo—on the better-sure-than-sorry principle.
Another—a young Sydneyite, Carter—has also sold to
Elliott for £3OOO ; and Elliott bought “ Big Ben ” for
£2OOO. The holders are now four—Elliott, one-third
share; Huxley and Mills, one quarter share each: and
Gardener, one-sixth. Will the gold go down? That is
the great question. If the reef extends 100 feet at the
present rate of richness, says the Telegraph special, the
Londonderry is worth a million of money. Anyhow, the
show is good enough for £25,000 to be offered and refused.
• • « *
Even Bayley’s finds itself of secondary moment
in the glare of the Coolgardie find. The Sheriff still holds
on to Cosgrove's Bayley’s South; Shaw’s South Extended
reports very rich gold in the parallel reef, worked from
the surface. The Emerald mine at Yalgoo has been
bought by a Maorilander named Stanley, of Dunedin, for,
it is said, £15,000 and one-sixth interest.
• * ♦ »
Zeb. Lane, writes a Bulletin correspondent,
does not seem to have got into the very best hands in
London. The city, by-the-way, is full of plausible fellows
who could talk a duck off a pond, and who profess to be
able to do wonders in the shape of raising capital. “ You
give me the option of the property, my boy/’ they say to
an owner, “ and I’ll eat my hat if I can’t find capital to
the extent of £lOO,OOO in 24 hours." The bait is taken,
and then these “ hawkers ” run the rounds trying to find
someone to deposit £lOO, or £2OO, caution-money, the
result being that the property they are trying to finance
gets known as a shady one, and no one in the end will
touch it. I don’t say that this is quite the crowd that
Zeb. has fallen in with ; still, he hasn’t had the luck of
Austin, who shaped differently, 'with the result that his
trip was a great success. Any really good Westralian
mine, worked or unworked, has a good chance of being
floated just now, but Bayley’s itself would prove a frost if
taken in hand by any of the noble army of “ hawkers.”

Wyalong (N.S.W.) is a drouthy field. Ib
already possesses 11 pubs, open, with several more in
course of erection, while every third or fourth house is a
sly-grog shanty. It has six “ working-men’s clubs ”
some of them boozing-kens and gambling-dens in an
easily penetrable disguise.

up, the other day, about J-mile S.W. from the Day Dawn.
A portion of the stone is of extraordinary value.

A large exodus of diggers has taken place from
the Murchison to Lake Carey, about 300 miles S.E. from
Cue. A party of prospectors arrived for provisions, with,
it is said, 300 oz. Hence the rush.
* « * •
Dear Bulletin, —The reports about the fever at
Cue, and other parts of this field, have been much
exaggerated. During the past five months only 18 deaths
have occurred from that and other causes. The climate
is most healthy and exhilarating.—O.H.T., Cue, 12th
June, 1894.

The telegraph-line will reach Coolgardie within

• • * *
The promoters of the Murchison Battery Co.
erected a splendid 10-head at Cue, and have since spent
about four months vainly seeking for water, which they
should first have found. Now they are considering the
advisability of shifting the plant, being unable to procure
sufficient water to work it. Day Dawn South Battery Co.
have got plenty of water, having been flooded at 70ft.
They will shortly be crushing for the public, to the great
joy of the smaller mine-holders.
• ♦ • ♦
An influential committee has been formed to
promote the driving of a crosscut across Bendigo at right
angles to the lines of reefs so as to intersect the innumer-
able small lodes which are known to exist. As the work
partakes of a national character, Government is to be
asked to help. Bendigo district is a network of reefs,
and the crosscut will discover lodes which no number of
shafts would find.
• ♦ • ♦
The prospectus of the White Feather Pro-
prietary Gold Co., printed elsewhere, is worth looking at.
The Bulletin hasn’t seen the show itself; but it has read
the expert’s report. The public are only asked for
£6250, and £6250 goes to the company’s credit, the
vendors being satisfied to take their price in shares—a
healthy sign. The names of the directorate indicate that
investors will get a fair run for their money.
♦ • * »
The Day Dawn, Murchison, is erecting another
10 head of stamps. At present it has only 10 heads
on a 14-foot lode, having been unable in the past to get
enough water for more than 10 heads.
* * * •
Mills’ Day Dawn United, Charters Towers,
early in January, ’93, found the reef in the main underlie
shaft, at a depth on the incline of 2000 ft. from the sur-
face, to be no less than 19ft. thick ; it has subsequently
been found to reach even 26ft. Since then the reef has
maintained its great size, and the quartz has improved in
richness. The output for 1893 was 24,048 tons m excess
of that for ’92, and is now proceeding at the rate of 4000
tons per tnonlh with no signs whatever of diminution. In
1890 the ore averaged only 15dwts. per ton, in 1891 it im-
proved to ISdwts., in 1892 it increased to loz. Odwt. 2grs.,
and in 1893 the average per ton reached loz. 2dwts. 9grs.
It pays nearly £lOO,OOO a year in diva, (on a capital of
£300,000), and looks as if it would last 20 or 80 years OB
the Day Dawn reef alone.

Competition. No. 15.
The above closed with 8333 guesses, which, at Is.
each, made a total of <£4lo 13s. The problem
set was as follows “ The jurymen in the Mont-
gomery-Williams case having found the prisoners
guilty, the latter were sentenced to death, accord-
ing to Botany Bay law. The jury subsequently
petitioned for the men’s reprieve, on the ground
that they did not believe Montgomery and Wil-
liams meant murder at all. The jury’s verdict
was not in accordance with their belief, but it was
in keeping with their oath, which was to find a
verdict according to Botany Bay law, the brutal
and law which drags a man into
the jury-box and, offering him in such cases as the
present no alternative between hanging and
acquitting, really compels him to commit
either murder or perjnry.” The missing
word, “ INDISORIMINATING,” was sent in

My Dear Mookabinda—■
Tempe ball was a whirling spectacle this year,
crowded with an exceeding crowd and beautifully
stage-managed. The rather difficult floor of the
Centennial Hall was worked up to perfection (it
was like a ploughed field at the F. A.L. ball), and
a very special feature was the poultry supper in
the basement, set on tastily-decorated tables and
tented round with bunting. The Tempe ball
supper was the best-done charity sandwich-
struggle of the season. And it was served up
before 11 besides—a proof of how much ammuni-
tion the committee had laid on. The worst thing
about the hop was the way in which the hall was
dolled up. Big, untidy bows of flimsy rag and
paralytic greenery are not my notion of wall-
gardening. The V.R, elevation wasn’t more
hideous than usual, only that a big card with the
name of the lending firm printed large made the
Satronesses look as if they were camping on
lahogany and Co.’s exhibit at a furniture-show,
just to demonstrate how the dais looked “on the
figure.” But the whole of the decorations wanted
straightening up~for instance, the rendezvous all
hung like train-signals when a puff-puff’s coming.
Gov. and Lady Duff were not on view, out of
(purely official) respect to the memory of M.
Carnot, but all the “suite” were there, squiring
pretty Miss Duff (in black, and carrying a ton-
weight “committee” bouquet) and her sister,
looking sleepy in pale-pink. Mrs. Bowden-Smith
wore a brocade gown. Mayoress Manning was in
pale-yellow brocade, and big posy of yellow roses,
and Mrs. RobertPrendergast in jet-sprinkled black
gown, with large damask roses on the corsage.
Mrs. W. F. Brennan was half-hidden by her
bouquet; Miss Annie M'Donagh (one of the hon.
secs.) wore a modish turn-out of sunlight-yellow
poplin, with lashings of real lace, and huge posy
of yellow roses and Czar violets.
The fancy-set dressing wasn’t very capricious.
Miss Ida Gilles cut the nicestfigure in the daffodil
set (all black gowns, poudre hair, and Directoire
sticks adorned with daffodils). The Red Riding-
Hood set (got up by Throsby Robertson) was


With reference to a statement in its recent
article on the late S. A. Agent-General Bray, The
Bulletin is assured by the deceased gentleman’s
law-partner, Mr. Haokett, that the late Agent-
General never at any time owned any interest in
the S.A. Advertiser.
