bright and novel, though one of the little Red
Riding-Hoods looked more like the wolf than the
heroine of the tragedy. The autumn-leaves square
was quaint—but the men spoilt the fancy sets, as
they almost always do. Unless you can scare up
four uniforms (with something inside ’em, of
course), or a quartette of conceited youths, who
will face a critical world with deep crochet lace
round velveteen knickerbockers and Cavalier hats
on one ear, you had better renounce fancy seta as
vain things, for the waiter costume is enough to
blot out the “ fancy ” aspect of comic-opera chorus.
Mrs. (Dr.) Mailler-Kendal wore a very Frenchy
costume of white brocade and satin, the wide
re vers effectively edged with brown fur ; hair
poudre and bouquet of roses; Mrs. “Dick”
Richards, in cream bengaline and Irish lace, was
neatly groomed, but the place was peppered with
impossibilities. Imagine any maiden hanging a
bunch of dead violets under her offside ear with
poor, dear Ophelia as a warning ! Or a charmer,
m white, with one white plume on her collar-bone
and another in her hair, like a cockatoo that has
moulted down to two feathers. Giddy-looking
bon-bon costumes, too—all golden filagree or
glittering scales—had a good innings, and anti-
cipated pantomime draperies, but not the least
amusing things were the society maidens sitting
on the shallow steps of the dais, like swagmen on
a kerbstone, with their knees doubled up to their
chins, gazing thoughtfully at the festive scene.
Some people would sit on the bare boards to be
in the magic circle of the vice-regal rendezvous,
but persons envious of social distinction shouldn’t
sit on the vice-regal step—it is so suggestive of
the step-up they’re yearning for.
By the way, I think the much-followed fashion
of fancy sets is about the best way to keep your
particular team in hand in the labyrinths of a big
public ball, that has been discovered up to date.
Not even the most assured outsider would take up
his position in a square to which the damsels in
whimsical attire were setting steadily in. Even if
a stray pair did wander that way the arrival of a
couple matching all the other couples standing
glowering upon them would make them flee from
the spot. Fancy sets are not always beautiful to
look upon, but they have one invariable virtue—
they always keep your “party” quite distinct
from the other people, and you can merrily dance
with one another all night long. In a society
seamed with little cliques this separate system of
dancing will continue wildly popular for aeons to
come.
A thing I notice at public balls and “ sich ” is
that when you round a corner and come plump on
Phyllis and Strephon it is nearly always a young
man who is caught red-handed, as it were. 1
suppose the moral is—do not “ sit out ” with any-
body under 40 (I am speaking to “ women only,”
of course), for the young man has not been
preying on society long enough to know the no-
thoroughfares of public buildings, and he sports
with Amaryllis, not in the shade, alas ! but under
a 40 horse-power electric glare, although there
may be an ocean of gloom and potted plants round
the next bend. I have never—emphatically never
—caught an oldster imprinting a salute upon
mademoiselle’s ear-lobes, or doing anything to
make the groundlings giggle as they pass. Mature
manhood doesn’t amuse itself to amuse the public,
too, but it doesn’t miss the fun of the fair either.
Therefore, O you many maidens who are prone to
being hugged by many adorers between the dances,
pause before you trust your sentimental fortunes
for the evening with a mere seedling Lothario !
The “Flowerland” Exhibition and Art Union
eventuated at Sydney Town Hall on the 28th ult.,
in the presence of a large slice of local fashion
with its best bonnet on. The Mayor of Sydney—
he of theLoNDON J ournal legs—opened the festival
with an exceedingly diplomatic speech, and then
the proceedings resolved themselves into an un-
equal sort of concert and everybody knew the
worst. Thirty works of art by “Flowerland” and
ten ibudents were plastered about the platform;
there were four stalls for photos, flowers, and so
on, mostly run by friendly Potts Pointers, and
the hall was decorated fore and aft with bunting
and greenery. In short, the festival was some-
thing like a musical “at home,” with prefatory
excuses made for it from the platform and price-
tags on the light refreshments. The concert
programme was strong. Madame Charbonnet-
Kellerman (in graceful mourning for M. Carnot)
Maude Heather Orlando Fitz-Stubbs,' Jessie
Middleton, Ed gar’cello Straus and others, all gave
their valuable services, but gloom had settled on
the audience and only a blast from the Last
Trump would have made the crowd-sit up.
As a show, “Flowerland” Festival was lady-
like and harmless enough, but I must rise and
object to the precedent. It was not a success,
however, and I don’t think any spectacle organised
by a more or less deserving person for her own
exclusive benefit would ever cub the purse-strings
of a discriminating Sydney public to any great
extent. If the world were in the habit of putting
a penny in the slob every time a fellow-creature
tooted his horn in an appealing key, every burst
boob in the land would charter the nearest barn,
corral the services of all the good-natured talent
in the vicinity, and run a one-night show for all the
small change there was in it.
Talking of benefits. The presentation to the
well-loved Mr. Alfred Dampier, of a cheque for
nearly £2OO and a handsome address, took place
at the Hotel Australia, last week. Mrs. Dampier
and her two daughters were present, in neat
serge gowns, and Mr. Dampier, in a Byronio
necktie, was visibly affected as the assemblage
drank his health in variously-split sodas. Mayor
Manning (by the way, the ballet needn’t trouble
to wink at his stage-box—he avowed, in a lovely
speech, that he had never been attracted show-
wards by the charms of the gold-washed chorus!)
did the presenting, and most of the flattery. I
liked the speeches, and I only objected to the
stress laid upon Mr. Dampier as a father. The
triumphant way they alluded to him in that
capacity looked as if it was a rare and original
thing to be a he-parent. Perhaps if Mr. Dampier
hadn’t been so devoted a parent—and husband—
he’d have been a bigger theatrical success. It is
a terrible handicap to firmly believe that one’s
ducks and ducklings are swans.
At the concert of Sydney Quintette Society,
sensation was lent to the classical bill by Madame
Kellermann and M. Poussard playing Beethoven’s
“Hero’’march as a tribute to M. Carnot—the
audience standing the while. The march had
been cleverly scored (considering it was done in 48
hours) for violin and piano (as the “ Moonlight’’
sonata and the “Kreutzer” had been arranged),
and everybody enjoyed listening to it —saving one
irrepressible newspaper lady. She tore behind
the screen, and her loud unabridged objections to
the introduction of the fiddle kept the front seaters
on the grin for the rest of the evening. It sounded
as if the soprano and the contralto were having it
out at last.
I have thankfully received a household cookery
by Mrs. H. J. Samuell, of Sydney. There are
many recipes—some for preparing sustenance in
the Jewish fashion, and I made myself quite
hungry reading it.
I learn that a Northerner who lately attained
his majority, and received about <£loo,ooo in hard
cash from his guardians and trustees, has been
frightened off to Japan—by the importunities of
the society-girls. According to an eminent Q. 0.,
one year in the Japanese tea-houses makes almost
any young man absolutely love-proof.
Yours affectionately, Sappho Smith.
Melbourne Confidences.
Melbourne, 3rd July.
The gilt-edged suburb of Hawthorn has been
enlivened by a double wedding. One of the
brides, a “Master of Science,” has committed an
intellectual mesalliance, her bridegroom boasting
only the inferior degree of B.S. But love makes
bold, and the maiden, having put her hand to the
plough, will not turn back. Romeo, who is an
albino, and has money, is, moreover, the holder of
a travelling scholarship, so his new owner intends
to bear him across the briny without loss of time.
Fostering his mental growth in some collegiate
nook, with bracing logic and mathematical tonic,
she hopes ultimately to raise him to her own level,
and behold him stamped with the M.S. brand.
The learned pair were nuptialed in academic
garb. The bride —a fresh, sensible-faced young
woman—looked no less attractive in cap and
gown than her younger sister in conventional
white silk and orange-blossom.
Western district was enlivened, the other day,
by the marriage of an undowered maiden to a
wealthy young squatter from another locality.
The bride was once ordered out of a ladies’ wait-
ing-room by a short-sighted railway employe who
mistook her for a man because she wore an ulster
and deerstalker cap and leaned her head against
her mother’s shoulder. The porter spread the
story of his idiotic error, and the victim awoke to
find herself famous. “Society” moralists made her
the text for disjointed sermons against divided
skirts and cigarette-smoking, neither of which
vices has anything to do with ulsters or felt hats
—or putting your head on your ma’s shoulder.
However, a squatter husband is consolation for
having one’s sex misinterpreted by a casual
observer.
Male visitors to the Austral Salon have a lovely
time, notwithstanding its rarefied atmosphere and
the intense mental culture of its members. In
fact, a noticeable feature of the meetings is that
wherever a man, married or single, may be, there
are the women gathered together.
A Melb. journal, regarded as the mouthpiece of
the cult, its editress being Highpriestess of the
“Temple of the Block,” vicariously flatters the
Salon’s pet politician, after this fashion : “ Mr.
Y.’s sister was there. She is very like her
brother, with the same clear features, bright eyes
and dark hair growing low on her forehead.”
How they do notice! And how awkward it
would be for a bald-headed man! Then, “Mr.
Y. is very properly adored by his family and by
the fair sex generally.” How’s that for incense?
Miss Aimee Moore, one of the Salon girls
(known to the sisterhood as “the Handmaiden”
from her devotion to the Highpriestess), is at
present in Sydney on the theatrical tack. She is
a rather pretty young woman of the slim and
meagre type, who, in dress and demeanour,
assumes a cross between Ophelia and Madge
Wildfire, her chief impersonations so far.
The Austral Salon sent out invitation cards for
Monday last, to commemorate—l had almost said
celebrate—the joint demises of Adam Lindsey
Gordon and Madame Lucy Chambers. It wasn’t
the date of the poet’s death, and the songstress
had only been buried a week or so, but as the
Salon, so it avers, exists to recognise talent, it
frantically grasps at every ghost of pretext. The
cards were chiefly noticeable for their funereal
appearance and want of grammar. The poet
rushed tragically out of life in a fit of despair!
and the event 1 is commemorated by a crowd of
women partaking of tea and tarts. Who says that
women aren’t humorists ?
An Orient liner brings back to our shores the
wife and child of Melbourne’s solitary Genius.
I hear from some compagnons de voyage that the
melancholy, so characteristic of the famous
“Idyll,” is faithfully reproduced in the weird
nocturnes of the infantile traveller. Heredity has
much to answer for.
History repeats itself, and the daughter of a
much-married Bendigo magnate has been led to
the altar by a widower with a young family. The
wise man guessed and calculated, no doubt, that
a wife who had herself experienced the changes
and chances of four stepmothers, would be a
sympathetic parent to his children. A pleasing
peculiarity of the paternal manage is that mothers-
in-law, past and present, are constant and welcome
guests, the climate apparently suiting themjbetter
than it agreed with the daughters gone before.
Melbourne, let me tell you, boasts of a well-
known professional man who has been married
thrice, and at a recent Govt. House ball people
were amused to see him handing in his three
mothers-in-law in succession to supper—especially
as he seemed to be on equally good terms
with the whole lot.
Ada Crossley arrived, with an ulcerated sore
throat, amid bleak, black weather, in London, in
the first week in May; but her prospects were
too bright to be dimmed by trifles. She writes to
a friend that she immediately set off in quest of
new clothes. She took 10 trunks of elaborate
clothing, parting gifts from admiring friends, so her
desire for more would seem to be mere exuberance
of spirits. She took also 96 letters of introduc-
tion, and books and musio enough to fill her com-
partment of the train when it left Spencer-st.,
Melb. But, apart from her magnificent voice, she
deserved all she got—she has worked hard, and
in the height of her success was never ashamed ol
either her poor beginnings or belongings.
Some friends of mine were at a big dinner in
London lately, and two ladies next them were dis-
cussing singers. “I admire Albani’s singing,”
said one, “but I like that Australian—l forget
her name—much better.” “So do I,” replied the
other, “bub I cannot think of it, either; I believe
it begins with ‘M.’” “Oh, now I remember,”
said the first speaker, “Orleans.”
Basso Magrath, of Belle Cole’s Co., was, while
in Melb., much admired by womankind, by reason
of his good looks, fine voice, and delightful Hiber-
nian humour. Mr. Magrath, however, is a Bene-
dict, and his wife (who, by the way, is a Victorian
native) is, during her husband’s absence, residing
in Ireland. Spada only the other day lost her
mother, while the Murkens’ husband (who resides
in Deutschland) is reported to be dying. Conse-
quently, the last Melb. appearance of these artists
were made under circumstances of sorrow.
All the Australian Governors read The Bulle-
tin, but Gov. Robinson is the only one who openly
delights in it. This story, told by himself, hangs
on a Bulletin par. Some time ago, he published
a song called “Remember Me No More,” which
The Bulletin said it couldn’t do. Recently an
opera co. performed in Perth, and one of the
vocalists, as a compliment, interpolated the song.
She was supposed to be clad in one of those
costumes that are slit up the side of the skirt to a
certain extent —sufficient to display a decorous
amount of limb. But the skirt of this lady, all
unknown to herself, was slit up a great deal too
far, so that on one side she unconsciously displayed
a pair of funny little blue-cloth trunks. Gov.
William says that as he looked at her and heard
her singing the refrain, “Remember me no more,”
he couldn’t help thinking of that Bulletin par.,
and said to himself, “ Can’t do it, Dora 1”
A festive item of the “Helping Hands” pro-
gramme (the H.H. being a strolling company of
charitable amateurs under command of the L. L.
Lewises) is the skirt-dancing of the Misses Hors-
fall, sister of Mrs. Rupert Carington. The
younger one, decidedly picturesque in white
muslin and pink silk, her face framed in a fluffy
cloud of dark hair, manipulates her skirts after
the most approved stage method. These damsels
visit the theatre solely to obtain Terpsichorean
wrinkles, and only the other night they were in a
box at the Princess, their eyes glued to the ballet.
A frisky matron, sick and sorry of compli-
mentary mourning and rural exile, is once more to
be seen in our midst. Perhaps the farewelling of
a stage lady killer hastened this return, the lady’s
devotion to opera being notorious; yet what might,
at one time, have been a fwrench, was borne with
apparent equanimity. ’Tis said, our sex dresses,
not to please the men, but to spite other women.
On something the same principle, perhaps, the fair
patroness’s tactics seemed designed to ostenta-
tiously welcome the coming , rather than to speed
the parting guest.
The announcement of a Williamstown girl’s en*
gagement recalls an elder sister’s matrimonial
triumph, which raised the whole dan from com-
parative obscurity. The fair victrix of yore,
while on a Northern visit, hung so reverentially
on the sayings of a middle-aged gold mountaineer
that he succumbed on the spot, and his slice of
the golden mount became a joint property in less
than no time.
The guests at a gorgeous wedding-breakfast,
who overflowed four rooms at Menzies’ recently,
were “ shadowed ”by private detectives. These
gentry, though incognito, brought a distinct im-
pression of spectre bridegroom into the affair, and
speculation concerning their business on this joy-
ful day has been rife.
At Carlton, lately, Mr. Isaacs charmed the ears
of female suffragists, and won a smile from his
pretty wife with the statement, that “from the
cradle to the grave, existence without the dear
creatures would be unbearable. ” He had hardly
finished, when a callow youth bounded on the
platform to reiterate the dreary chestnut that
women were best employed in guarding the baby.
Then an ancient stepped into the breach with the
brilliant suggestion that “all ladies ’adn’t orf-
springs to mind,” and the callow youth, rendered
reckless by excitement, said—“ Well, they ought
to 1” Which was rather embarrassing for the un-
married delinquents.
A certai i Victorian damsel is commiserated
for the supposed disappointment of her
father’s will. The document, I’m told, was
never intended to do more, so far as she
was concerned, than endow her with a handsome
home —house, grounds, live-stock and ready cash
thrown in—as by her late mother’s marriage-settle-
ment of .£50,000 (besides accumulated interest) she
was already provided for.
A parcel of new music from Ricordi and Co.,
the Italian music-sellers in London, includes a
pretty “ Harem ” waltz by Bucalossi (with a
rather giddy frontispiece), and at least one
catchy Tosti song, called “Sorridimi” (Smile on
me). The words of this love-lorn ditty are a
translation from an Italian bard, and will suit any
passionate tenor who meditates doing another
mash. Another intensely Italian ballad is turned
into “Only to Dream you Love me”—music by
Francesco Quaranta. In this case eight lines of
warm verse are spread over a full allowance of
warm music on the anthem principle.
VAN M.
At Poverty Point.
There is a touch of unintentional humour in th«
sombreneas of “ After Dark." It is where the fatally-
beautiful Scot Inglis, as Gordon Chumley, meets his
former fellow officer, drunk, tattered, grey, bald, dirty,
and utterly stone-broke, carrying a sandwich-board, and
filling in his spare time by trawling for suicides in the
Thames, and after weeping over him and talking of
bygone times gives him—of all things on this earth—his
card. Then, for the first time in many drink-sodden
years, the other man realises the full measure of bis
degradation, and he puts the pasteboard aside wilh his
palsied hands because he feels he isn’t worthy, where-
upon his friend clasps him in his arms, and begs him
almost with tears to take his card. Finally the hungry
wretch takes the card and shuffles feebly away with a
determination to reform. The card has awakened him to
a desire for better things. It is a blessed thing, when a
man is hopelessly wrecked in this stony world, to meet a
sympathising friend who will give him his card.

It was pitiable to see poor old Nellie Farren carried in
an invalid-chair to Irving’s box, at the Lyceum, one even-
ing lately, to sample “ Faust.” However, barring being
crippled, says a correspondent, Cinder Ellen did not look
much the worse for wear.
It is said in London, remarks The Bulletin’s corres-
pondent, that Mrs. Langtry absolutely cried when she
went off the stage after her cool reception in “ A Society
Butterfly.” Fancy the Lily in tears! But what else
could she expect? When it was announced that she
would appear in one of the tableaux as Lady Godiva, the
public could not stand being put off with a figure in a
high-necked frock. The Lily must learn to live up to,
and dress up to, her reputation.

ago, is now with the Bland Holt Co. at Sydney Lyceum
under the stage-name of Adelaide Hope.

ing and sending Miss Dudley to Australia.
Dan Barry, who contemplates a season at Sydney Royal,
is having “ the wind taken out of his sails ” by Rignold,
who is rapidly producing pieces to which Dan has
“ pinned his faith ” for years.
Lonnen, writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent,
is quite eclipsed by Sheridan in “Little Christopher
Columbus.” As the detective Hooligan, Lonnen, of
course, has to assume a brogue, which went down well
enough with those who are not judges until Sheridan
came along with the “rale” article. “Columbus” still
draws marvellously, but more owing to May Yoh6’s two
songs—“ Lazily, Lazily” and “Oh, Honey, my Honey,”
which are always rapturously received. There are people
who go night after night to hear her. Yet she has a most
peculiar voice, which can only be described to that of a
very well-taught raven.
Bill Holloway has taken Terry’s Theatre, London.
Vanity of vanities I
Tub Bulletin regrets to notice the bankruptcy of Mr.
Arthur Garner, theatrical manager—liabilities, £52,970 ;
Assets, £54,687. A few years ago Mr. Garner retired from
business a rich man, but he has come down in the common
crash.
“Jack” Holloway, the only son of Plain Bill, has gone
to France to learn the lingo, prior to studying law. He
leaves the stage to his sisters, Julie and Thea.
All Marshall Hall’s malignity against the critics arose
through one of that ilk having made a venomous attack,
not on his music, but on a favourite though ferocious dog
during the days of his early “ colonial ” residence. He
then lived at the quiet, boom-born suburb of Moreland,
and until his dog was poisoned he was quite kindly dis-
posed towards everybody—even the critics. He resented
the outrage on his canine friend by leaving the suburb
where the dog had caused a reign of terror, and removed
to the domicile sacred to the memory of Hamilton Clark—
which latter abode proved equally uncongenial.
Signorina Orelli, as Oasilda, was the real success of
“The Gondoliers” amateur show by Melbourne Opera
Club. She is the youngest girl of the company, has the
easiest stage manner, and certainly the best voice of them
all. With a more becoming costume she would have been
unanimously voted superior to Florence Young, as
Florence was when she first attempted the same char-
acter at the Princess’. Miss Orelli is to “come out” in
opera presently, they say, and, what’s more, she is calcu-
lated to remain out when she gets there.
When is “Haddon Hall” to be produced in Australia?
The “ firm " promised it before their last Melb. season,
but now it seems as far off as ever.
What with Sass’ attempt to complete the marriage-
contract, in the “Life of Pleasure," and the tropical
scene between the King of France and the virtuous
heroine in a nightdress, Balfour and the W.C.T. Union will
soon be bringing in a bill to suppress the Princess
Theatre altogether, and strew the site with salt.
A younger son of playwright Tom Robertson was con-
victed of reckless fraud, the other week, and sent to
Melbourne Gaol. Latest London papers report that
T. W. Robertson, the eldest son of clever “Tom,” lay
dying of brain-paralysis. Insanity and crime are brothers,
and genius is often their father. Tom Robertson’s chil-
dren were born in his struggling Bohemian days, before
the Bancrofts had produced “Society,” and put him on
the road to his short-lived prosperity.
Miss Fortescue is "towering” with “Hypatia,” the
provincial rights to which she has purchased. Her
devoted Vanderfeldt is nothin the caste, and the part of
Issachar (says The Bulletin’s correspondent) is played
by Murray Carson, who (if it is not treason to say so)
realises the character, both in looks and acting, much
better than Beerbohm Tree. Vanderfeldt is under engage-
ment (theatrically) to Miss Fortescue for several years,
and he has been loaned during the tour of “Hypatia.”
He is still as spoony and devoted as ever to the rejected
of Gumboil.

Miss Annis Montague (Mrs. Charles Turner) will, on her
return from M.L., open a school of music and singing in
Melb. The talented lady taught Prances Saville, Edith
Moore, Tom Gordon, Cecily Staunton, and some 14 or 16
others. Although she did not bring Melba “ right out”
—for Cecchi was the diva’s tutor proper—she taught her
several very useful “wrinkles” which Melba has not for-
gotten to acknowledge.

Murderer Bertrand left all his paintings
and nic-nacs behind him in Darlinghurst Gaol-
being unwilling to take away any memories of his

Vic. Attorney-General Bryan O’Loghlen
has never been known to make a joke or put a

Ex-sculler Trickett mounts guard at the
Moama Customs end of the Murray bridge. He
has developed into a morose official automaton
of the most aggravated type.
Three S.A. Speakers are dead. Of these
two—Kingston and Bray—passed away at sea,
while the third (Ross) is believed by some to have
committed suicide in a fit of depression.

of 75 members— i.e. sons and daughters of Queen
Victoria, grandchildren, and great grandchildren,
and the various consorts of the married members.
The Calvinistic world will bracket Carnot’s
assassination going to the theatre on Sunday, with
Lincoln’s, at the theatre on Good Friday. But
glorious Waterloo was fought on the blessed
Sawbath.
M‘llwraith, many people will regret to
hear, is suffering from dropsy. Last week Jimmy
Tyson walked out to see him but was denied ad-
mission to the sick room. Tyson is deeply con-
cerned about the ex-Premier’s health.
C. A. Wittenbach, the clever Melbourne
journalist, drowned in the River Winnipeg,
Canada, was for seven years law-reporter of Melb.
Age. His disappearance from Melbourne was
mysterious. One night sub-editor Stephens won-
dered why the law-copy hadn’t arrived. Next
morning Wittenbach didn’t turn up and a message
was sent to his house. He hadn’t been there since
the previous morning. A year rolled by and
nothing was heard of him till news of his death
came from Canada. Wittenbach, who was of
German parentage, was one of the most amiable
and best-loved men on the Southern press.

That man of vim, Pastor Isitt, had £275
a year as a Wesleyan parson in Maoriland. His
income as a pump-priest is much heavier. At
Napier he made .£4O in a week’s lecturing, and his

Mrs. Charles Meredith, the veteran Tas-
manian authoress, is back in Melbourne after her
late trip to England. At some years over 80 she is
as sprightly as ever, and is now actively engaged
upon a new book, her originality and productive-
ness being by no means worn out.
There still lives in Daylesford (Vic.) Evan
Jones, a bronzed naval veteran, who was awarded
a medal for saving life at the wreck of H.M.S.
London in ’39. Two years ago he married a
young wife who lately presented her lord with
what Parkes calls “a couple of sweet souvenirs
of wedded affection.”

rise in the world mostly become Morphys.

From London correspondent of S.A-
Advertiser :
When the first cablegram appeared saying that Play-
ford had been appointed S.A. Agent-General, poor Bray
let his paper fall and said—“ Cruel, unkind.” Nearly
the whole of the rest of the day he kept his eyes on the
paper, and his thumb was placed just below the telegram.
Of the many influences which moved the
Australian-born Bishop Green into the episcopacy
of Grafton and Armidale, N.S.W., it is under-
stood that not the least active was that of those
who were prepared to go bail that the young man
would stand up for the traditions of the “ grand
old church.” He is doing it. All native Austra-
lians want are the back seats when Englishmen
are about.
Poor old Tawhaio, the Maori King, now
spends much time in selecting temporary wives
from the bevy of fat and pleasing wahinc that
attend upon royalty. When not engaged in being
married, he concocts “royal proclamations,”
which he issues from his own special printing-
press. These proclamations cover pretty nearly
everything, from an ordinance upon straying pigs
to dissertations upon life in the next world. His
last proclamation was one forbidding any Maori
girl to wed a pakeha husband.

celebrated the jubilee of his trial with O’Connell
and others at Dublin for “sedition.” This time
50 years ago Duffy was “ doing time ” as a result
of the trial. Duffy is the sole survivor of the
eight accused. The eleven counsel who prose-
cuted for the Crown and the sixteen barristers
who appeared for the defendants — likewise the

The Ministerial head of one Australian
railway department is cordially hated by every
officer from the Commissioner to the engine-
cleaner. Even his own colleagues will not speak
to him unless compelled. On one occasion, throw-
ing round a roll of papers, he remarked to the
head of a department, “ See to this matter, or”
here he lifted and swung his foot suggestively.
Another time when new lines were under discus-
sion, he remarked to the assembled engineers and
surveyors: ** Pooh ! my wife could do all tho
engineering required on this line, and my old gar-
dener all the surveying.”
George Louis A. Davies, formerly hon.
sec. of the N.S.W. Farmers’ Association, and now
secretary of the N.S.W, Press Association, one of
the dozen or so Parliamentary candidates for
Willoughby (N.S.W.), is a printer by trade, and
was the first man to establish a penny newspaper
in Sydney. It was a semi-weekly, and proved
snob a success as to cause moneyed imitators to
establish the daily Evening News, which knocked
the semi-weekly out.
An old Lachlan River paafcoralisfc, beloved
for his fairness to station-hands and his kindness
to swagmen, absolutely abhors the sound of cattle-
bells or the ticking of a watch. He has been
known to get up in the middle of the night, saddle
a horse and yard teamsters’ bullocks to take off
their bells; and would run, on foot, half a night
after a horse, through the scrub, for the same pur-

A Bulletin correspondent quaintly gossips
about Isaac Pitman :
I was a pupil of Isaac Pitman at Wotton Underedge in
Gloucestershire (Eng.) in 1836. He then taught us short-
hand on a black-board. He was a tall man and parted
his hair in the middle. He was full of energy and walked
at the rate of five or six miles an hour. He was very
much liked hy his pupils—he gave them lots of outdoor
exercise, ami was in the habit of taking bags of apples to
the top of Wotton Hill and rolling them down for the
boys to scramble after. His school was in the first flat of
an empty cloth-mill; his brothers Benjamin and Henry
helped him in the school. Mrs. Pitman devoted a lot of
her time to teaching poor people to make soup and to
making the best use of the food they had.
Sophia, the well-known Maori guide at
Rotorua, who took the Governor and party around
the other day, complains that, although Glasgie
expressed his approval” of her intelligence, he
didn’t part a cent. There was once a Scotch
Governor of Fiji, who, having been informed that
it was necessary for him to make a slight return
to some big Viti Levu chiefs who had sent him a
handsome present, replied that he believed in
making a return present of equivalent value, and
sent them six tins of pressed mutton, a broken
breakfast-set, and an axe-handle. There was also
once aN.S.W. Governor who, while_ visiting the
Northern rivers, met an old aboriginal identity
who claimed local kingship. “How much ought
we to give him?” enquired His Ex. of the Mayor.
: Oh, say five shillings,” replied the Mayor.
After a confab with his suite, the Governor sub-
sidised His ]Vla»j£sty to the extent of kikpen .
Dear Bulletin, —It’s not at all certain, as
you seem |to suppose, that Victoria’s Chief Com-
missioner of Police will be replaced by a ranker
to wit, Superintendent Thomas I arkinson, of
Ballarat. He has fairly earned the position, but
will have to fight for it with Fire-low Price,
Evans (Inspector-General of Prisons), and several
others who are in the field. Parkinson s would
undoubtedly be the most popular appointment,
and if experience counts for anything, the best.
The difficulty, however, is likely to be postponed
by the retention of Mr. Chomley for a further
term. That step would be most favourably re-
ceived of any, as the silver-headed old Chief is
idolised by the “Foorce,” appreciated by the
powers, and trusted by the public. Just, generous,
courteous, capable, handsome, and humane!
That’s Chomley, and long may he reign !—Yours,
Cop.
A shark, 15ffc. long, and weighing a ton,
has been captured and landed at Kiama (N.o. VV.).
This is the shark that swallowed Jonah. A portion
of that prophet’s camel’s-hair girdle was found, in
a remarkable state of preservation, in the monster s
stomach. .
Premier Kingston is much annoyed with
Speaker Coles because he believes it was through
his betrayal of Play ford’s confidence that all the
details about Agent-General Bray’s illness were
made public property. As a matter of fact, how-
ever, Playford, freed from restraint because of
Kingston’s absence in Westralia, babbled about
the matter to everyone he met, being quite unable
to contain his joy at the prospect of grabbing the
vacant billet of Agent-General with its <£lsoo a
year.
Minister Frank Suttor, of N.S.W., was
born to be a knight; and is now “right on the
job.” Cable; —
Hon. F. B. Suttor, the delegate for New South Wales,
proposed the adoption of an address to" the Queen con-
gratulating her Majesty upon the 57th anniversary of her
coronation. In his remarks he laid special stress upon the
vast growth of the colonies during her Majesty’s reign.
The address was adopted. The members of the Conference
sang the National Anthem, and the spectators joined in.
How very transparent!

The S.M. Herald one
day recently gravely informed
its readers that Mr. W. W.
Tarleton had been admitted
to practise at the Norfolk
Island Bar. We may be told
next that Purves, Q.C., has
found his way to the Bar of
Kangaroo Island.
Mr. James Brown Pat-
terson says that he didn’t want
a knighthood, and he regards
it solely as a compliment to
the province of Victoria. The
Bulletin hasn’t yet met with
onesolitary knight who wanted
his kirghthood, but they all
took it none the less. A title
is about the only thing that
everybody will take against
his will. Anything else that
a man doesn’t want, such as
a kick, or a piece of tainted
meat, or a glass of beer with a
dead fly in it, he will promptly
refuse, but he always grabs at
the title with extreme reluc-
tance, and seizes it desperately
with both hands. Then he
explains that he took it to
please his wife, or his Queen,
or his country, or his dog, or
something equally irrelevant.
This paper wants to hear of
one honest K.C.M.G. who
will say straight out that he 8
glad of the title for his own
sake, and that anybody who
tries to take it from him will
be killed in the struggle.
The Wilberforce (N.S.W.) co-operative
settlement has been split in halves—one to be
worked on socialist, the other on individualistic
lines.
The Bulletin has just discovered some-
thing, A correspondent:—
Now that N.S.W. Parliament has collapsed, the refresh-
ment-room committee should devote some attention to
the mysterious grog-bills of certain members. Legis-
lators, for years past, have shown a marvellous reluctance
to pay their scores. A member would invite half-a-dozen,
or so, of his constituents into one of the smoking-
rooms and ring for the waiter. On the grog being handed
round the cowed and courteous flunkey would pause
for cash or “instructions.” He almost invariably
received the latter, which amounted to a quiet request to
“ Put them down to .” And the waiter would
reply with meekness and assumed innocence, “Very well,
sir.” And accordingly the grog was scored—“ Sir -— ,
, per Mr. Jones-Brown, M.P., six drinks.” A
reference to the score-book reveals a colossal and ruinous
bill against for drinks for which he never will
account—and never should. This little frivolity has
found most favour among Protectionist and anti—
members of the Assembly. The obedience of the waiters
is the essence of the playful imposition upon the credit of
the political Patriarch.
Said correspondent is standing for Parliament
himself, and if he has any luck, as The Bulletin
trusts he will, he intends, next session, to have his
drinks also put down to Sir Blank.
Neuralgia
is the first, surest and most reliable sign of nerve
decay; it is a symptom provided by nature to
warn the sufferer of approaching weakness and
distress, though in most cases the pain is purely
local; the disease is constitutional, and local
remedies, such as liniments, oils and menthol,
only give temporary relief and do not do the
slightest permanent good, for they only act by
deadening the sensibility of the affected nerve.
The remedy, to be of any lasting benefit, must be
tissue forming and nerve strengthening and must
have general invigorating properties ; it must also
contain the material to restore the lost tissue to
the impoverished nerves, and no remedy except
Clements Tonic possesses these wonderful powers.
Clements Tonic has been used for thousands of
cases by young and old of both sexes for neuralgia,
sciatica, and other nervous complaints, and we
have never heard of a case where it has failed to
give quick and prompt relief. Head the case of
Mr. W, H. Stigings, 8 Bute-ter., Newtown, who
writes ori December 20, 1892; —Having for a long
time been suffering from nervousness and languid
feeling, also frequent dizziness, I had been under
medical advice, but not deriving any benefit
therefrom was advised to try Clements Tonic.
I did so, and after taking four bottles I can say
conscientiously that it performed a thorough cure.
I have none of the old symptoms from which I
suffered for the last eight months, I therefore
recommend it to everybody.—l remain, yours,
etc., W. H. Stigings, 8 Bute Terrace, Newtown,
Sydney.

POLITICAL POINTS
N.S. Wales general election —next Tuesday
week, July 17.
The people who pray most for the Queen
prey most on the country.
A Referendum Bill will be introduced, by
Stout, during the present session of Maoriland
Parliament.
West Tamworth (N.8.W.) elector: “Are
you in favour of interprovincial freetrade?”
Premier Dibbs : “ No.”
The Royal Commission engaged on the
consolidation of the N.S.W. statutes will cost the
country at least £20,000.
Should Dibbs miss his footing at Tam-
worth, and a Protectionist majority be returned
v at the polls, Lyne would aspire to the N.S.W.
Premiership.
N.S.W, grog-secretary Bevill objects to
women getting the franchise because they would
be too much influenced by their spiritual pastors.
Bevill is a spirituous pastor.
The N.S.W. Railway Act provides that
Ms.L.A. shall hold their free railway passes
“ until their successors shall have been elected.”
What a bulge on the other candidates !
“ Cease, viper, you bite a file ! ” was
Carter’s enraged response to some recent Shiels
criticisms. Carter really regards Shiels as an
adder, and always finds a sting in his tale.
A resolution condemning the Govt, was
moved by Stout in the M.L. House of Representa-
tives last week, and defeated by 48 votes to 19.
That doesn’t look as if the Ministry was weaken-
ing* ,
Where is Premier Dibbs’ Sinking-fund
vanished to? Apparently it has gone where
Mount Huxley went, and where all the other
dead things go. Nobody ever hears of the great
scheme now.
A couple of N.S.W. political anomalies.
Freehill, a Protectionist candidate for one elec-
torate, signs a requisition inviting M'Millan to
stand in the Freetrade interest for another elec-
torate; and H. H. Cooke, a Freetrade candidate
for Condobolin, signs a requisition inviting Stokes,
a Protectionist candidate, to stand for Ashburn-
ham.

on which death-duties
can be honestly de-
manded is as a return
for the protection and
safety afforded to the
dutiable property by
the laws, &c., of the

impudently claims to
make property here
contribute to make
good her Budget defi-

has mostly sprung out
of military expendi-

no benefit whatever.

more garrulously pla-

as at the Mayor of

perfect training-
ground tor the floor
of Parliament How
many eminent parlia-

Benjamin Short, parliamentary aspirant,
says that “the Creator of the Universe is a free-
trader.” Is Short a Christian? If so, does not
the Christian Book tell us that Jehovah had a
“peculiar people,” a “chosen seed,” and was
therefore an out-and-out protectionist ?

Prof. Morris, of Melbourne, suggests
(“ after ” an old Bulletin picture) that every
speaker, speaking in public, should have a rope
round his neck, and be summarily strung up if he
offered to orate for more than an hour. Well, men
have been hanged for less.

asked for, ‘it is too much;’ when anything is
granted —‘ it is enough.’ ”

“Honest” Tom Playford, S.A. Agent-
General, as befitted a practical ex-pumpkin-
grower, seems to have been the only delegate at
the Ottawa Conference who talked common-sense.
Fancy hears the pudding-faced burly man
throatily exclaiming—“ The purpuss of tins yere
Conference is to advarnce trade between the
dependencies, and not to deal with matters ot
mere sentiment.”
“ Elections aren’t now what they used to
be ” remarked a reminiscent Sydney produce-
dealer one day last week. “ I haven’t got a single
order for a case of stale eggs yet. I mind the tune
I stood for up-country—that was before 1 went
into the produce business —the whole electorate
smelled of bad eggs. Candidates now-a-days don t
need money, don’t need reputation, and don t need
nerve. And you call that advancement?”
Gangs of time-expired N.Q. Kanakas are
combining to cut cane by contract, and pay white
men a percentage on their contracts to nominally
represent them as contractors. The Kanakas up
North also lease farms, to which Northern capi-
talists strongly object, ostensibly because these
farms are hot-beds of vice; in reality, however,
because these leaseholds make the Kanaka in-
dependent of the planter and enable him to
demand a decent living wage.

South Australia is pressing for “ a rather
lengthy interregnum ” before the appointment of
a successor to Gov. Kintore. _ This is right y
recognised in Downing-street circles as a move
towards the abolition of subsidised figureheads.
For if a province can do without a Governor tor a
time, what is to prevent all the provinces from
doing without Governors for all time? Nothing,
replies Australia.
A disaster like that of Pontypridd in
Wales proves that employments generally
reckoned as mere manual labour ones often merit
remuneration graded on high professional lines.
When coal-miners demand wages which will leave
a surplus over their keep employers throw the
character of their work in their faces ; but their
work goes hand in hand with death, and this tact
should be paid for out of the profits.
N.S.W. Trades and Labour Council gives
up the ghost on July 1, to make way for the
Labour Federation District Council scheme.
It’s offspring, the Labour Electoral Leagues,
should, logically, cease to be at the same time. It
is more likely, however, that the “bosses ’ of that
organisation will start the “Don’t swap horses
when crossing a stream” cry, in their own
interest, as nearly every one of them is a candidate
for Parliament.
Freetraders go regularly to the shrine of
Parkes, and twirling a sheet of paper between
finger and thumb complain of the insolent
familiarity of Reid. The old man wags his head
but gives them nothing they can quote. If, now,
some of these irate interviewers could but mount
a public platform and wag their heads just as
Parkes does, giving at the same time an accurate
account of the complaints made, there would be
renewed friction among the heads of the party.
“Jack” Murray, member for Warrnam-
bool, was the only Vic. M.P. who ventured to
sneer at the loyal ecstacies of J. Brown-Patterson
re the birth of “ the future king of England.’
Whilst he lamented, with mock seriousness, the
fact that the child, in not being born in Australia,
had missed the honour of being the godson of
J.8.P., Speaker Bent spluttered in positive
anguish, and the other members looked as if
expecting a thunderbolt from God to strike dead
the man who dared be other than crawlsome in
speaking of a baby prince.
“ The Samoan Commission,” says the
Samoa Herald, “ has now restored hundreds of
thousands of acres of land to natives, from whom
it had been taken under claims set up by
foreigners. An idea of the grasping disposition
of foreigners may be gathered from the fact that
the land claimed by them amounted to 2,000,000
acres, while the total amount of land in the whole
group is only 900,000 acres. About 200,000 acres
will be decided in favour of foreign claimants,
and 700,000 will go back to the natives.”
“ We may well pray, ‘ Lord, forgive us our trespasses,’
for it’s no good asking God to forgive our blunders.
(Hugo).
The appointment of a Tuesday in place of
a Saturday as the day on which N.S.W. general
elections are to be held, is not likely to rob many
workers of their chances to vote. But it is calcu-
lated to arouse their resentment, and to inflame
their determination to vote no matter at what
hazard. Further, the infliction by an employer of
any sort of punishment upon an employe, for
absence from his work in order to record his vote,
should be made a punishable offence.
Queer that Cardinal Moran should be
turning Carnot’s death to church profit in
Sydney, by inviting every notable in N.S. W.,
from tile Governor downwards, to a magnificent
requiem mass, “which is to partake of a national
characterwhile, at the same time, the authori-
ties in Carnot’s own France hold a secularised
celebration in the Pantheon which the clergy will
not attend. The Latin races have lost faith in
clerical intercessors ; the Teutons, slower to
believe, seem slower also to relinquish the ritual
of belief.
Four provinces publish rough estimates of
revenue and expenditure for the year to June 30.
Westralia is the only one which comes out on the
right side, with a surplus of ,£28,000, which, added
to previous credit balance, puts the province
,£90,000 to the good. Victoria shows a woeful
leak, revenue falling short of expenditure by
£530,000; while the nominal deficit is now
.£1,784,048, and the real deficit, with the Shiels-
Berry Treasury bonds added, is somewhere about
2,i millions. South Australia and Queensland
expect to just about balance.
Included in S.A. Govt, policy
is the Lake Bonney irrigation
scheme, to be run on co-operative
lines under direct State control.
Premier Kingston hopes thus to
absorb the shaky village-settlements
on the Murray, several of which
must soon collapse unless State sup-
port is accorded. The Lake Bonney
land, under irrigation, would be as
productive as Renmark, and supe-
rior to Mildura, according to ex-
perts.

Dear Bulletin, —The argument thatther e
should be no profitable industrial prison-labour i s
a boomerang with a wicked recoil. Following out
that principle in its integrity, the free man out-
side, who has not been found out in his illegalities)
would have to keep in idleness the caged man who
was stupid enough to be caught, and would have
to pay for the latter’s board and lodging.
The erection of gaols is an expensive luxury.
Prisoners should undoubtedly be required to earn
at least their keep, besides enough profit to pay
interest on the cost of gaol-buildings, and to pro-
vide each released man with a small sum of money
upon his exit into the world again. When will
some philanthropist arise, powerful enough, not
only to promulgate, but to see carried to a success-
ful issue, some scheme by which all but the more
desperate and violent criminals might be “boarded
out ” as are children from Industrial Schools ?
There are, of course, labour-market difficulties in
the way, but a convict’s labour should be as valu-
able as a free man’s, and his wages should be the
current rate of the market. All that the present
denouncers of prison-labour are justified in is to
demand that prison labour-products shall not be
permitted to undercut free labour-products’ prices.
—Dipso.

through a horsehair-collar. Its ancestors mostly

Has the wit of man ever invented a plot or
situation which has not its duplicate in real Id®?
The chief Tanqueray complication arose through
the mother’s old lover turning up to woo the
daughter. Now it is cabled that Madame Trebelh-
Bettini, the famous singer, disinherited her
daughter because of a quarrel with a similar cause. .
Miss T.B. wished to marry Mrs. T.B.’s ancient
flame, which Mrs. T.B, (jealous, no_ doubt)
would on no account permit. Miss per-
sisted, and Mrs. left all her money to
the Academy of Music. Here you have
Binero’s idea in all essentials, if not in details.
And doubtless such a set of circumstances has
often been arranged. For what could be more
natural than that the qualities which of old
attracted a man in the mother should re-assert
their dominion in later days in the daughter ?
• * *
Vic. Legislative Council made a gorgeous
display of “loyalty” and lunacy in honour of
York’s baby. Sargood, the knighted draper with
a genius for putting his foot in it [“ Philopene!
Mr. Sargood] said the event was of special
interest to the Hempire, “ because of the
cloud that fell across the life of the mother
not long ago”—a sweet, delicate reminder that
May had consoled herself for the loss_ of her first
lover by giving him a nephew after his decease !!
James Service said the event was an advt. for the
system of Government under which the Hempire
flourishes. It was a glorious system. Every year
the opponents of the system grew “fewer and
fewer.” Once upon a time, quoth pawky James,
the people dreamed of a republic, but now they
perceived that republics “ had entirely failed to
give the comfort, solidity, and protection to life
and property which obtained in the grand old
mother country.” And Service was in London
not so very long ago, in London where ‘ the
spread of discontent is so rapid as to astonish
Anglo-Australians who make periodical trips
home !” But, after his Commercial Bank speeches,
one can believe Service capable of saying any-
thing _ „

Sydney Mail announces, among its illus-
trations for June 30, “ The Birth of a Prince.”

In the sleepy town
of Napier (M.L.) the
council has ukased that
“ every person bathing
in the sea, or in any
river or other water
within, or within one
mile of, the boundaries
of the borough of Na-
pier, shall be attired
in a decent and proper
bathing-dress extend-
ing from the shoulders
to the knees, no white,
or flesh-colour, or net
garments to be worn.
Which law is so beau-
tifully comprehensive
that no man can legally
bathe in a tub in his
own house now within
a mile of Napier unless
fully dressed.

It has arrived ! In
a St. Kilda family
(Vie.) there are two
sons and two daugh-
ters. The girls go to

liners, whilst the sons,

The other day a member of an old Aus-
tralian family who, if he had done anything, had
criminally assaulted his servant, and had originally
been charged with that offence, was indicted for
common assault only. Which illustrates for the
thousandth time The Bulletin aphorism that the
lash is for the workman, and that no broadcloth
coat or stove-pipe hat were ever hung up at any
set of British or Australian triangles.

* 1 Thanksgiving services for the safe accouchement of
H.R.H. the Duchess were held in all the churches on
Sunday last.” —Daily Paper.
A diary, kept by the man supposed to
have been murdered, is a feature in the 1 raser and
Mrs. Anderson case, Melb. In the law-books
there is a divorce-case where the wife, respondent,
had committed the folly of_ writing a minute
account of her philanderings in a diary which fell
into the husband’s hands. The obvious defence
of the co-respondent was that this could only be
the outpourings of a diseased imagination, but
the jury held otherwise, and the diary was fatal to
the defence.

The Adelaide foot-police used to be allowed
off duty one Sunday in six, but retrenchment has
cut down their numbers so low that this con-
cession has been withdrawn, and they have to
work continuously from year’s end to year’s end
—Sundays and holidays included —save for a fort-

night’s leave annually, which is equal to getting
14 Sundays out of 52. The mounted constables
stationed in the country, however, get enough
holidays to make up for the deprivation of their
city brethren, and, as a sidewalk patroller put it,
they “slape in their beds ivry night of the day.’'
Much complaint is now heard concerning political
interference with police discipline in Adelaide.

When Sara Bernhardt was in Adelaide,
Lord Kintore wrote to Wybert Reeve, asking
that the vice-regal box should be reserved for him
and his party each night of the season. The effect
of the reply was: “Yes, m’lud —at £5 a night.”
Whereupon came the announcement that his Ex.
would only be able to get to the theatre twice
during the stay of the goddess. It makes a
difference, don’t it ?

we would keep on searching there.
» # m
Dear Bulletin, —Your portrait of the
trous—beg pardon, skirtsers—has aroused a
furore of interest. How does she get them on?
Does she pull them on over her feet —as we do; or
put them on over her head, as they do (I mean, of
course, the other sex) ? Do they button on to the
waistcoat or does she wear braces ? Are there
pockets, if so side or front ? And can a man find
them when the garments (I suppose it’s plural
number) are hanging in a dark wardrobe? Are
there buttons or hooks and eyes ? Or safety-pins
or tape ? Do they tuck up at the bottoms on wet
days? Everybody wants details. —Makkied.

They gave her a wide ” berth, ! butshe stood aloof,
evidently quite unconcerned.

* • *
The police are credited with a lot of
cleverness re the detection of the Barraba mur-
derers, Cummins and Lee. As a matter-of-fact all
the credit is due to good old “ Information Re-
ceived.” A third criminal, who was to have had
a share of the plunder, when he found that there
was no plunder, gave the whole thing away.
From the report of Barraba murder trial
Harry Tan was a very nervous man, and scarcely knew
what he was saying. He was half aboriginal and half
Chinese, and was a boundary rider.
Mr. Tan recalls a French gentleman up North
who had learnt English from blackfellows and
Chinamen. He might have known what he was
saying, bub few others ever did. For instance,
when he one day wanted to say that it would be
well to catch two horses across the creek up the
range before it began to rain, Monsieur Desqua-
ville remarked : Plenty suey tout-a I’heure. Baal
your chercher yarraman over creek longa mooltan
tableau me myself.”
# # *
Good old Dr. Neild, discussing the Mel-
bourne poisoning case with a newspaper reporter,
said that even if the analyst were to entirely fail
in his search for arsenic that would be no proof
that Louis Juncken did not die from the effects of
arsenic. Poor Mrs. Needle ! though they were to
dig up a hundred of her dead friends without find-
ing poison in any of them, it would be no proof
that she didn’t kill the whole lot, we suppose.
However, it is stated that Analyst Blackett has
found in the body of the deceased Juncken enough
arsenic to slay him.
* • •
Councillor Anderson, the chief champion
of electric-lighting for the city of Adelaide, died
suddenly on 23rd June, and the gas-directors are
trying to look sorry. Anderson was once engineer
of the S.A. Gas Co., and was well qualified to
speak on both sides ; he was engaged on a series of
lectures to the citizens when seized by apoplexy.
His party feel staggered by his death. Not long
ago he was divorced from his wife, who consoled
herself with somebody younger, and his gas-
shares, with their income of .£5OO a year from
divs., were among the property which passed over
to her under her marriage settlement. Therefore,
the gas-directors sneeringly said he wanted to run
down their value.
« • *
Owing to a double drought—lack of rain
and cessation of subscriptions—the Finke (German)
mission station in the Macdonnell Ranges (S. A.)
has been abandoned after supplying food, cloth-
ing and piety to the aborigines for half a century.

springs for wire mattresses.

Labour member M ‘Gregor, M.L.O. (S.A.),
hurt Basedow’s Teutonic feelings by affirming in
the Council the other day that though there is not
woman’s suffrage in Germany there is plenty of
women’s suffering. “That is a slander,” inter-
jected Basedow, and thereupon M‘Gregor told a
true story about a young constituent of his who
wished to marry one of two daughters of a Prus-
sian farmer resident in S. A. “ Ach, nein,” cried
the eater of sauer-kraut, “she cannot marry
until grubbed and cleared is der whole of my
farm. Yen dat is done, poth of them can you
marry if you vant her. But if you dakes dwo you
feeds dwo.”

and the envy of the men. Up North, long since,
the writer saw him marry, there being no
parson on the field, a long digger to the c mu ly

Folks of all degrees,

That she isn’t dead, but living
Perfectly at ease,
After bringing forth a “ stranger,”
Doctor says she’s out of danger—
Singularly odd !

Who allowed this lucky woman
To perform a function human
Like a common clod 1
Oh, it’s very odd.

Praises be to God !

Baby’s looking well :
Not contented with arriving,
Babs is positively thriving—

Doing such a thing ?
Ickle future king!

DOMESTIC ECONOMY!
WHEAT IS IT ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY is primarily the art of feeding and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
without impairing the family digestion, or taking away the character of its members for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic economy is not comprised in buying a bad article for sixpence when better can be
got for sevenpence ; but if the ruling price for the best is eightpence, and somebody comes along and offers it for
sevenpence, then it’s domestic economy to close with the merchant so offering. By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich oni s richer. What say you ? Will you do your business at

It is pretty tough to ask people to believe
that a long-sentenced prisoner, lately released
from Darlmghurst prison, had 300 sovs., the result

“JOE” EDGE, the cross-country horseman.
“Joe” Edge, whose photo, appears above,
started his riding career six years ago, as an
amateur, and proved an immediate success, inas-
much as he then steered his trusty nag Saxon to
victory in the Maiden Steeple at Warrnambool.
He was still “Mr.” Edge when he rode Fearless
11. in the Maiden and Grand National steeple-
chases at Elemington, running away with both
races as though there were no other horses in the
field. A while after that big double event the
smart young amateur took up his quarters in
Sydney, and had fair success “ across country.”
Returning to Victoria he shifted into the ranks of
recognised professionals, and it was as plain Joe
Edge that he annexed last year’s big steeplechase
with Trojan. A first-class rider and a clever
tactician is Joseph—a very keen Edge, in fact.
His portrait has particular interest at the present
moment, for there are weighty whispers abroad
to the effect that Edge’s mount, Bill, will possibly
capture the V.R.O. Grand National Hurdle race
on Saturday next. Certainly the subject of this
notice is worth “following” over the sticks at
meetings big and small.
* » »
Guy Boothby, the “ great Australian
explorer,” tells a story, in the Idler, about a
Governor’s wife and a policeman. A ball was in
progress at Government House, and the lady,
coming across a trooper standing guard at a door,
told him that supper would be provided for him
and the rest of the force on duty in the servants’
hall. Then she caught a view of his face and
found that he was her long-lost brother, an ex-
officer who had vanished from England in dis-
grace, several years before. “ What the sequel
was,” says Boothby, “ I never heard.” The most
obvious sequel was that the lady went back to
the ball-room, and that her long-lost brother
remained on duty at the gate, and that nothing
happened. Nothing ever does happen under such
circumstances. There is no sequel. It is only in
fiction that the prodigal son comes back and is
taken in. In real life he returns to find that his
mother has died and his father married again,
and then his father died and his second wife
married the local preacher, and the old man’s
executors sold the fatted calf to the butcher.

straightforward style. If Justice Madden wants

and beard, and dog the footsteps of _ certain
notorious individuals who converse with jurymen
elsewhere than within the precincts of the court.

On the appeals of several residents, the
police of a certain southern suburb decided to
raid a “disorderly house” with the intention of
arresting the regular inhabitants as keepers, and
the casual visitors as aiders and abettors. Said
raid came off all right, but it was not a success,
and the officers had to put up with a present of a
£5 note apiece instead of the new “stripes” calcu-
lated upon. Eor, foremost among the “ aiders
and abettors,” was one of the best-known Judges
in the province.

* * •
The wife of a Melb. editor lately had an
adventure with a supposed burglar, but who was
more probably a mere offensive drunk. He walked
into her house at night and sat down in the
drawing-room. The lady entered; “Mr. ’s
not in,” she undiplomatically said. “I know,”
was the answer, but the visitor made no attempt
to move. The hostess was scared now, but kept
her presence of mind, and, running to the study,
telephoned to the office that a burglar was
in the house. On hearing the message the man
fled, and a quarter of an hour later the husband
and a couple of detectives were scouring the sur-
rounding gardens for him, but unfortunately with-
out success.

The tendency to go asleep at Mr. Arthur
Galton’s literary lectures is spreading, even
ladies falling under the spell. When women nod
during a lecture performance, it indicates that
man and matter are both “off-colour.”

The law doesn’t always prevail. A young
Sydney man dangerously shoots a widow. After
weeks in hospital she recovers, but disfigured for
life. She refuses to prosecute, and accused is dis-
charged. A girl hacks the throat of a man who
jilts her, but he recovers. He refuses to prosecute,
and she is discharged.

Any counsel for plaintiff in a libel or
slander case who has the impudence to announce to
the jury that his client will not pocket the pros-
pective damages but will distribute them in
charity, should have his verdict, if any, upset and
be compelled to pay the costs out of his own
pocket. This kind of thing is bribery and cor-
ruption in its very worst form—bribery and cor-
ruption in the Courts of Justice.

A writer in Sydney D.T. complains of the
imperfect character of Australian art criticisms.
The lady is quite right. Criticism and art are
twins and grow together. Australian art criti-
cism is not a bit better than Australian art.

Bunny, Robinson, Fox, Longstaff, Corider,
are Australian painters who excel in the Paris
Salons, for 1894. Again rises the question why
the Australian National Galleries, notably that of
Melbourne, are so poor in French art ? Why do
not our young artists go for their education to
London, if English works, as a rule, are alone
worthy a place in our galleries? Why, the Royal
Academy, compared to the French Salons, is as
the moon unto the sun.

Dear Bulletin. —“ Wurlie” is somewhat
mixed, seeing that “Brigham Young” and not
“Smith ” was leader of the seceding party who went
in for polygamy. The Cape Hawke, N.S. W., people
are honestly monogamist. They have to be—they
haven’t enough females to go round twice. The
seoeders tried to convert the Maoris some time
back, but without success, as the existing sensible
sexual customs made polygamy superfluous.

One Smith, British Consul at Samoa,
lately made a luminous suggestion to the Samoan
Government to prevent men deserting to the
rebels :
Upon the suggestion of Mr. Cusack-Smith, the British
Consul, an army regulation has been adopted providing
that any one who deserts shall, upon capture, have the
word “Coward” tattooed upon his chest, a stigma the
fellow must carry to his grave. Tattooing a man thus
when ho changes his politics, after the manner of
Samoans, is not likely to make the cause of the Govern-
ment popular.
This Smith is the gentleman who, among other
serviees to the cause of civilisation in Samoa,
introduced kid gloves. But, after all, he is a man
of ideas. How we wish we had tattooed Dibbs
when he was a Republican—and Parkes when he
was a Protectionist—and Stout when he was
Plain Bob -and Griffith when he was a Democrat
—and MTlwraith when he was an “ Australian
Nationalist 1”

The “open” or “barred” witness-boxes
in the new Melb. Law Courts were adopted
because Chief Justice Stawell, cross examiner
Purves, and other experienced lavvyers said that
when a witness lied he was uneasy in the legs and
knocked them about. Some perjurers, they said,
had wonderful command over their faces. The
Bulletin has noticed that many men who have
gone into lying as a business wear hair on their
faces —it is so hard for a nervous liar to manage
his mouth. But when a professional liar has full
confidence in himself he boldly shaves clean.
(Most lawyers are clean-shaven). The clean-
shaven liar who can really manage his mouth is a
copper-fastened practitioner and A 1 at Lloyd’s.
* # *
A Bill to abolish juvenile smokers has
been introduced in Tas. Assembly by that recon-
dite humorist, Dr. Crowther. It provides that
“any constable may lay hands on any male or
female child under the age of 16 years found smok-
ing a cigar or cigarette in any street or public
place, may take away the cigar or cigarette, and
demand the child’s name and address.” A second
offence may mean 24 hours’ imprisonment. There
the Bill stops, regarding a third offence as un-
thinkable. The constable is to be sole judge of
the “ child’s ” age, sole chaser of offending urchins,
and sole reporter to parents, who may obtain the
child’s release on demand.

Four distinct charitable organisations carry
on their operations in Sydney without any refer-
ence to one another’s doings. The consequence is
that one person is sometimes helped simul-
taneously through four different channels, while,
as there isn’t enough to go round, another person
is not helped at all.
“ Tom Collins ” returns to the charge :
Dear Bulletin, —In my par., of June 16, 1 inodistly
held continence to be an important factor in fitness for
social survival, wi I h the necessary corollary, that “the
higher the ‘ moral tone ’ of a community, the better that
community will fight for freedom. “Titus Salt,” after
noticing my pure ignorance on a point of history, invites
me to build a coherent theory on certain facts supplii d
by him. In justice to myself, I may say that, as written
by me, the historical summary ran: “Sparta deterioratid
morally, and went down before Macedon, the most moral
state then existing. The latter deteriorated,” &c. But The
Bulletin, which knows everything and allows no one
else to know anything, pruned the passage not wisely,
but too severely. The Lord reward it according
to its works. Now for our facts. (1) In applying the
titles, “ hollow, garish, immoral” to virile Macedon,
“T.S.” unconsciously mixes two eras. My qualification
was strictly relative, and historically correct. (2) The
moral Ironsides defeating the mors moral Scots. Trivial;
but, in any case, Dunbar stands conspicuous as a generals ,
not a soldiers’, battle. (3) The Household troops of
Louis XIV. : These met less immoral and more formi-
dable soldiers in the sons and grandsons of the
Ironsides. Witness the terms of the Peace of
Utrecht. (4) The Janizaries: Well? Vher is dot
barty now? The point at issue is—morality in its
relation to survival. (5) The moral Puritans of New
Plymouth, and the immoral companions of Cortez :
Compare Massachusetts —which (says Prof. Nictiol of
Glasgow) has produced more thinkers than all the U.S.
territory south of Mason and Dixon's line-wilh Mexico.
(6) Henry of Navarre: Trivial again. Good man gone
wrong—provided for in former par. (7) Who said that
“ Sparta never saw a foreign enemy at its walls for 460
years?” Everyone except “ T.S.” knows that this was
the only capital of antiquity which had no walls. “T.S.”
misses Plutarch’s idea, which is, that from Lycurgus to
Agesilaus no foreign enemy obtained lodgment on
Laoonioan soil. “Many of your countrymen lie on the
plains of Thebes,” said the Theban. “ But, none of your
countrymen lie on the plains of Sparta,” retorted the man
whose morality certainly bore closer relation to his
patriotism and prowess than to bis ignorance and dirt.
(8) Modern Greece—grovelling, bankrupt, and moral, I
p ass _but with the suggestion that no immoral people
ever survived foreign tyranny such as these have borne.
(9) So with the Irish.' Moreover, the latter exhibit a
racial vitality, a resilience, and general fitness for survival
equalled only by one other race on earth. That race is
the Jewish ; and however justly we may take exception
to the shape of Israel’s nose, we must concede his
relatively high morality. “T.S.” might have trawled the
stream of history, from the ancient Benjamites down to
the Templars, and so on to his Janizaries-from the
"giant judge” to the flower of Arthur’s mythical
chivalry, and on to the “uncrowned king ” with the
same melancholy result. However, the col,erent theory
built by fair induction on these facts is this: That as the
Order of Things won’t stand phallic-worship beyond a
certain indeterminate point, “moral tone ” is not merely
an important, but an essential, factor in social survival.
One factor, of course, implies other factors, such as ent, r-
prise and intelligence—lacking in Greece; and the
faculty of cohesion—lacking in Ireland. Also we infer
from our tacts that the ageless cult of Priapus is a thing
of failure and a sell for ever. Simply my own single-
handed contention till, like Paul of old, “I was com
forted by the coming of Titus.” Clad in impassioned
rhetoric, and armed with a peculiar two-edged logic, this
champion came—like Balaam on his Soudan—to curse,
and remained to bless. And to a noxiously and perhaj s
suspiciously moral man like myself, this is eminently
satisfactory.
We have received a most valuable little
book from the proprietors of Mellin’s Food,

Despite adversities, Messrs. Goldsbrough,
Mort, and Co., Ltd., retain their place as premier
Australian wool salesmen, having disposed of
120,159 bales from March 1, 1893, to end of
February, 1894, 10,000 bales more than were sold
by the firm next highest on the list. The firm’s
Annual Review regards the general prospect
hopefully. “There will be,” it says, “a
degree of recovery which, if it does not place our
commercial and producing interests quite in the
flourishing condition in which we have been
accustomed to regard them, will, at all events,
re-establish a confidence, the want of which, in
panic times, did so much to paralyse every enter-
prize.”
M’Burney, Wallis & Co.’s Report.
Sales which end the wool year were held this week, and
we are pleased to be able to report a slight increase in
prices on those rulintr at last fortnightly auctions.
Our latest news from London is also decidedly en-
couraging, and we think when the new wool comes down
a more cheerful state of things will prevail at our wool
sales.
The rise this week may be accounted for by the good
news from London, and the presence of buyers from
Melbourne.
Wool offered was of the usual class coming forward at
this time of year, namely, scoured, and odd lots of greasy,
and a clip or two from Queensland.
Although some slight friction has cropped up over the
shearing question we do not anticipate a strike. We hold
the opinion that past strikes have been, in a great
measure, the means of bringing about the long spell of
depression our colony has just parsed through, and a
strike now, when we can just see better times looming in
the distance, would be suicidal for all. We would urge
shearers to think for themselves and not listen to agita-
tors whose business it is to create strikes that they may
have a rosy time at the expense of the working man.
Sydney, June 29, ’94.


The Queensland Boot Trade.
Mr. John Hunter’s Brisbane factory.
For 25 years Mr. John Hunter’s enterprise has
not rested. He holds the premier position in his
trade, and it is an open question if there is a
bigger industrial manufacturing business south of
the line. First New South Wales, then Queens-
land, South Australia, Tasmania were in turn
selected as an outlet for Mr. Hunter’s commodi-
ties until “John Hunter’s City Boot Palace” is
a household word irk every Australian province
except Victoria.
Queensland is now on the threshold of an in-
dustrial greatness, and may soon be expected to
move prominently along the highway of prosperity.
The pioneers of industry in Queensland deserve
well at the hands of all true colonists. Mr. John
Hunter is one of the pioneers and it is fair to say
that he has from a protectionist’s point of view
shown what enterprise can accomplish. His aim
is to make, from Queensland leather, boots and
shoes to suit all the requirements of Queensland
people. His factory is turning out work rivaling
some of the finest that comes into the market, and
every month sees some improvement. The Aus-
tralasian boot trade is now but little dependent
upon the sweating markets of Europe; and in a
few years a French, German or Northampton boot
or shoe will be as rare in Queensland as the mega-
therium. Mr. Hunter’s factory stands on the
corner of Albert and Ann streets, opposite the
Wesleyan Church, and the building is one of the
most conspicious in Brisbane. The warehouse,
show-room, and counting-house divisions are on
the ground-floor, facing Ann-street. The offices
are well laid out, and the show-room is a perfect
paradise for those who love to be well shod. There
is displayed almost every conceivable boot and
shoe, from the J.H. nail blueher worn in mining
communities to the various fancy-makes in various
shades of Russian leather “tans,” blackened brown
canvass, white and buff and satin leathers in all
the best-known shades.
Leaving the show room and counting-house we
visit the press-room, where many sides of leather
per day are being converted into soles and heels.
The leather here used is all of Queensland manu-
facture. Next we view the “clicking ” room used
for cutting out the uppers; and here we are
enabled to examine some very fine leather
materials. Especially noticeable is some Austra-
lian satin kid, certain qualities being quite equal
to the best American satin leather.
Before leaving this floor we are shown some of
Corrick’s patent unbreakable backs, principally
for ladies’ and childrens’ boots, composed of a fine
strip of hard green hide, which prevents boots
from “sagging.” Asking to see about the best
value that Mr. Hunter offered the public, we were
shown some of his best make in the “ Federal
Brand,” which is stamped on each pair of boots
as a standing guarantee to the buyer. All the
latest improvements in boot-making machinery
are here in use, and with the improved quality of
fine Australian made leather, and excellent finish,
Mr. Hunter’s goods are pushing the English and
Continental boots clean out of the Queensland
market. Besides his numerous retail establish-
ments in Brisbane, Mr. Hunter has branches at
Ipswich, Toowoomba and Maryborough j and no
doubt ere long operations will extend to every
important town in Queensland,
AN EMINENT DIVINE.
THE REV. DE WITT TALMAGE.
HIS LECTURING TOUR IN AUSTRA-
LASIA.
IIiML
Through the courtesy of the Waterbury Watch
Company (whose Australasian head office is at 54
Margaret-street, Sydney), we are enabled to pre-
sent our readers with a portrait of the Rev. Dr.
T. De Witt Talmage, the famous preacher and
lecturer, who is so well-known as the pastor of
the Brooklyn Tabernacle, New York. Dr, Tal-
mage’s ideal vacation is, as the American terms
it, “a stay” at his new and beautiful country
home in Long Island, a few miles from Sag Har-
bour. But the rev. gentleman could not indulge
his fancy for more than two weeks this season, as
he recently started on a trip round tbe world with
his son, Dr. Talmage, of Pittsburg, as his com-
panion. During a portion of this trip the doctor
will be occupied in delivering a series of lectures
in Australasia, under the auspices of that experi-
enced manager, Mr. R. S. Smythe. He gave the
opening lectures of the series at Auckland on June
21. The wonderful Waterbury watches have
always been favourite timekeepers among the
clergy; and reasonably so, for the Waterbnrys
are durable, accurate, elegant, and inexpensive.
They rarely get out of order, and can be repaired
at a merely nominal cost. Henry Ward Beecher,
another eminent American preacher, was an
enthusiastic admirer of the Waterbury watches,
one of which he always wore. And G. H. Lea (of
Liverpool), the South African missionary, carried
a Waterbury throughout his recent journey
of two thousand miles in South Africa. “It
went splendidly the whole time, and .was twice
immersed in water without being injured.” Mr.
Lea also took with him a twenty-guinea English
lever, which went only two months from the time
it was purchased. Dr. Talmage lately celebrated
with his friends the 25th anniversary of the
beginning of his pastorate in the church which he
had made famous throughout Christendom. As a
leading New York contemporary observed the
other day : “ Dr. Talmage deserves allthe honours
that may be paid to him. During the last 20
years he has been a great power for good in the
world. Millions of men have heard his voice, and
tens of millions of both sexes have been brought
within the sphere of his influence by the printed
page. May he live to celebrate the golden anni-
versary of his connection with the Church of the
Tabernacle !”
SUNDRY SHOWS.

The Shakespearian comedy at Sydney
Criterion continues to exhibit all the various
features which were hinted at last week.
Also the improvement which this paper an-
ticipated in the Celia of Miss Moore
has not made its appearance, and that lady’s
performance continues to be as feeble and uncon-
vincing as hitherto. The Bulletin begins to fear
that it is not likely to be anything else. In addi-
tion to which its opinion of Mr. Greenaway is on
the down grade, and excepting Miss Hilda Spong,
whose Rosalind remains as lovely as ever, and
Mr. Leston, there is not one conspicuous success
in the show. As for conspicuous failures they
continue to stand up like the blighted gum-tree on
the Old Man Plain, and the two dukes, and the
exiled lords, and the wicked brother, and the
shepherds, and others are all vanity and vexation
of spirit. Last of all, The Bulletin has
a growing suspicion that Shakespeare him-
self is a delusion, a failure and a weariness
unto the soul, and that nobody really cares
for him, only everybody is afraid to express
his true opinion concerning him lest he should be
taken for a Goth and a literary Hun. Therefore,
everybody professes to love the dramatist who is
vaguely supposed to be Bacon, and almost every-
body is kept away by unexpected business, severe
cold, mumps, domestic bereavement and other
hollow pretences when the object of his adora-
tion is put on the stage. The Bulletin would be
glad to hear from persons who want to throw off
the yoke and avow their undisguised hatred of
the immortal hard—all correspondence on the
subject to be marked “ Bacon ” in legible letters
on the envelope.
