Mr. George Walton is leaving for America
—almost everybody is leaving, more or less, just
now—and the invariable benefit comes off .on
Friday afternoon at Sydney Criterion. The pro-
gramme is of the usual fragmentary order, and
includes the third act of “As You Like it,” by
Miss Spong’s Co., four comediettas, and various
other items, and all the companies now in Sydney
are represented in the cast. Mr. Walton goes to
New York, under engagement to Rice, whose
“ Evangeline ” somehow escaped its righteous
doom in this country some years ago.

Mr. Frank Emery’s benefit eventuates at
Sydney Majesty’s next Tuesday afternoon.
*- * *
Bland Holt’s impressive spectacle at Sydney
Lyceum concludes next Friday, and on Saturday
this notable artist breaks out in a fresh place with
“The Prodigal Daughter.” This is also reported
to be an affair of great magnitude; it is the one
joy of Bland’s life to tackle dramas containing
hundreds of tons of unmanageable scenery, and
only the biggest things obtainable appeal to his
soul. Meanwhile, the “Life of Pleasure” is finish-
ing its career in a highly satisfactory fashion, and
Henriettta Watson falls away from the paths of
virtue every night in her green dress and her
green hat amid much public commendation ; like-
wise, the fetching Miss Harrie Ireland (whose
portrait is given elsewhere in this issue)
escapes nightly from the affectionate villain
to whom she has just been married. The
reason why the heroine generally falls in a green
dress and afterwards repents in a brown one and a
sixpenny hat will probably never be discovered,
but The Bulletin notes with pleasure that Miss
Watson discards the brown dress tradition, and
repents in a pretty costume which is much better
suited to her character. Much of the badness in
this world is caused by the association of goodness
with a brown frock and a sixpenny hat.
* * * *
Owing to the undue enthusiasm of the
“ push ” in the sixpenny seats, George Rignold
felt called upon last week to raise the tariff at
Sydney Majesty’s. The sixpenny crowd—or at
least that part which was sixpenny by instinct—
has since left in disgust, and “ After Dark ” pro-
ceeds much more peacefully as a consequence.
The musio-liall scene permits of the introduction
of much fresh matter, and last Saturday there
was an entire new programme ; but otherwise the
drama preserves its consistently lugubrious aspect
unchanged. There are some powerful scenes in it
—notably the tunnel and the express train, and
the scene wherein Miss Watts-Phillips casts
herself, or her dummy, headlong from the flies
into the river—but it is as cheerless as a dead fish
in a tomb, and the moaning of the consumptive
person who sleeps in a gin-case on the bridge is a
mournful thing to hear. Nevertheless, the drama
is going to keep on its heartrending way for some
weeks longer.

Arthur Garner must have dropped money
at Adelaide Royal with his company. To
follow a leg-show with the legitimate in Adelaide
spells ruin, and Garner followed Musgrove’s
beauties. The only thing to do is to bring along
another leg-show, and Tommy Hudson at the

* * * *
Nellie Stewart achieves two specially
good bits of acting in “Ma Mie Rosette.” The
first is where Tapley points out to her that a king’s
intentions towards rustic maidenhood are never
strictly honourable. Her air of frightened inno-
cence, as she slowly “ tumbles ” to this royal fact,
is very effective. Again, when in the dream
scene, Nellie gives physical suggestion of love’s
“ mystic feeling,” her art is true to nature.
Presumably she has had the advantage of seeing
the part well played in London, or Paris, or in
both places, and probably her performance is an
improvement on what she saw, anyway.

* * * *
“The World Against Her” afforded
melancholy pleasure to a crowded Melbourne
Royal on Saturday night. In time past a lucky
stroke of advertisement gave notoriety to this
crazy five-act moan, wherefore the mention of its
revival summoned mourners from afar, like the
tolling of a funeral bell. 'Twas the signal for a
hearty burst of grief in store, and the patrons of
misery planked down their small silver at the
pigeon-holes as though they were buying tickets
for "East Lynne.” Miss Maud Williamson is
the weeping wife and mother, whilst Charles
Holloway is the unscrupulous contortionist
who chases her round with vile ends in view.
Her troubles begin early and end late, but
if they didn’t end at all the audience might
enjoy itself somewhat more thoroughly. To
be a complete success Miss Williamson would
require a lingering death in the last act sur-
rounded by the body of her long lost child,
and the villain would have to cry “ By Heaven !
she has escaped me!” when he discovered
her remains. However, with the exception of
its so-called happy finish, the entertainment
is very satisfactory. J. W. Sweeney makes a
realistic Punch and Judy proprietor, and Inman
curses his innocent wife with genuine enthusiasm.
Several other people wander amidst the woe to
more or less purpose, the least human of them all
being Miss Alice Deorwyn, whose method of
passing ordinary remarks illustrates the awful
difference between a dramatic career and any
other kind of professional occupation. Next
Saturday (7th) the company will introduce the
recent Sydney nightmare, “ A King of Crime.”
*
“Ally Sloper’s Night Out,” at the Alex-
andra, shows signs of having developed from an
orthodox farcical comedy into an abnormal go-as-
you-please. Probably it was once the ancient
story of a heavy British husband who shouts
oysters for a designing Tottie, and expiates his
crime by hiding in the next room whilst his lawful
wife takes refuge behind the curtain because her
character is somehow at stake. The Robert Brough
character of the original farce is here transformed
into the infamous penny fiend of imported
“comic” literature. Also the proceedings in
general are adapted to the aforesaid creature’s
code of honour, and the result depends a good
deal upon circumstances. There are ten comic
songs and a display of undisciplined young
legs in this startling event, besides a pathetic
ballad with a row of girls swaying to and fro in
the background. A show of this sort improves
as it goes along, and if Dan Barry can get it to
progress in the right direction it may suit the
Haleck clientele better than a football-match.
Daniel himself impersonates the bottle-nose and
boiled eye of fiend Ally. Miss Lilia Wilde
betrays a turn for broad farce in the part of
Tottie, and Martyn Hagan, Dora Mostyn, Lucy
Fraser, and the orchestra do their best. Nor is
the youthful “ballet” destitute of interesting
features, above’and below.
** * *
Afc the Princess’s a steady demand for
“ Ma Mie Rosette ” culminates each week in a
great Saturday rush. Nellie Stewart’s throat is
holding out against the weather, although her
stifled coughs have an ominous catch in them, and
she is apt to omit her principal song from the
second act. According to present arrangements
the opera will be limited to a few months’ run and
the last six nights are looming near.
** * *
The Bijou was packed full on Saturday,
for the first time in many months, a significant
circumstance that may lead to a permanent occu-
pation of B. and B.’s former camp by the cheap
but reliable minstrel person. Anyway, the Cog-
hill’s first appearance there, with a company not
newer or noticeably stronger than other crowds of
the ’all stamp, gave promise of a paying season.
Alhambra show, meanwhile, is calculated to bear
comparison, and the nigger at popular prices will
have a strong grip upon the city just now, when
horsey matters are in the air.
** * *
In a week or so that sorely-depressed in-
stitution, the General Dramatic Fund, will take
a benefit in the form of a costume football match
and incidental fair. The idea is to give a large,
open-air entertainment, probably at the M.C.O.
ground, in preference to the theatre matinee of
other years. Admission to the ground will be six-
pence, or a shilling at the most, and people
desirous of further assisting the good cause can
take out the price of their benevolence in a liberal
assortment of side-shows at threepence a time.
With fine weather granted the affair should prove
a big success.
The Hallewell Glee Club will commence a
series of Saturday night concerts at the Y.M.C.A.
Hall on the 7th inst. The fact of that old Sydney
identity, Mr. F. J. Hallewell, acting as musical
director, and Mr. W. H. Whiddon as business
manager, is proof positive that popular approval
will be well catered for. A photographic group
of the members, which includes some well-known
faces, notably Mrs. Riley, Miss Gibson, Messrs.
Hinchy, Barrett, Clancy, Hallewell, and that
promising contralto, Miss Louie Galvin, may be
seen at Messrs. W. H. Paling and Co.’s.
Alto glees are announced as a specialty.
The promoters purpose presenting at each
concert a short and select scene from several of
the leading operas, those specially singled out at
present being Mozart’s “ Seraglio,” the second act
of Gluck’s “Orpheo,” and scenes from “Iphigenia
in Aulis,” “Iphigenia in Tauris,” and Handel’s
“ Semele ” and “Hercules.” A vast amount of
time has been spent in rehearsals, and judging
from the extensive preparations made, arid the
combined talent of the club, these concerts should
prove a great success.
G. H. Reid, the harmless N.S. W. political
pug, who tries to look like a bull-dog, has formu-
lated his “Freetrade” policy for the severalth
time. According to his latest pronouncement, he
intends to raise, by customs and excise taxation,
“ about <£2,000,000 a year,” so it is difficult to see
where the Freetrade cornea in. Further, hia views
on land and income tax are as follows :
I point out that in the case of every owner of land up to
£2500 improved value the tax would certainly not be
more, probably less, than £4 3s. id. a year; and in the
case of an income of £lOOO a year, derived from personal
exertion, probably not more than £l2 or £l5; the Cus-
toms duties taken off saving more than £l5 a year to the
landowner paying £4 3s. 4d. in land tax, and leaving the
incomes up to £lOOO a year in a better position than
before.
Now the one absolute certainty of the case is that
the tax which is taken off one man must be put on
to another, and when G. H. Reid promises to
take £l5 a year off the person who has .£2500
worth of land the most probable inference is that
he intends to put it on to the person who hasn’t
£2500 worth of land. This fatherly care for the
man with £2500 worth of land is ominous
for somebody. The present N.S.W. revenue
from Customs, excise and other small items
of taxation is less than £2 15s. per head,
and G. H. Reid proposes to reduce it to about
£2—a difference of 15s. per head. But as the
landholder above-mentioned is to save not 15s.
but £l5 per head, it is evident that the duties to
be abolished are those on luxuries—the kind of
duties which press especially on the rich, or com-
paratively rich, classes. Therefore, the duties to
be left on must be those which press especially on
the poor, which is a joyous prospect for Freetrade
generally and for the poor in particular.
At Wyalong, the crowd, which is generally
expected to be noisy in a mixed community,
receive the candidate in a becoming manner, and
then vote want of confidence in him. It is proved
that in nine cases out of ten, the amendment is
preferred to the motion. The Labour Party,
therefore, lies low and at the finish pops out with
an amendment like this : “ In the opinion of this
meeting, Mr. Brown-Blue is a fit and proper
person to withdraw from his candidature and we,
the electors of Wyalong, request him to canvass
the district in the interest of the candidate selected
by the Labour Electoral League.” At the last
meeting the candidate (in the Freetectionist
ticket) provided himself with a proposer (his paid
secretary) and a seconder, who travels with him
in the buggy. These were the only support he
received, with the exception of three drunken men
and six others, who thought they were voting for
the amendment.— Scotty the Wrinkles.
John Tighe Ryan’s very latest (in yet
another interview with Parkes), in the Review of
Reviews :
Sir Henry lives like an English gentleman. [Hm !
Ed. B.] He observes the ceremonies of high life, and a
prince could sit at his table. . . . He is at his best
when the ladies are in the drawing-room.
No he isn’t. He is at his best when the ladies
have left the drawing-room. Our colonial oath !
Another slab :
“ I have never known ” (says Parkes) “ what it is to
feel envious of those more favoured than myself, and I
have never withheld my last shilling from those who
needed it more than I.”
Modest, benevolent old beauty ! Again :
“ That is the key to my whole life,” he added, “whether
people accept it or not—do you understand?”
Of course John Tighe Ryan thoroughly understood-
“l am not proud of the places I held. Anyone
could get into the same places. Look at Premier
Dibbs ! ” Oh the gall and the wormwood of it!
The hated Dibbs, who has held the N.S.W.
Colonial Secretaryship, Parkes’ prescriptive per-
quisite, for three long, infamous years !
The viper Dibbs, who was photographed
with the Prince and Princess of Wales ! and
who rubbed in the vitriol by sending Parkes (who
was never photographed with any prince or king,
not even a blackfellow) a copy of the photograph
aforesaid ! The Bulletin understands old man
Parkes and sympathises with the man from
Brummagem in his ill-concealed agony.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Squatter Manifold’s recent performer, Apsley,
is, at time of writing, the popular fancy for the G.N.
Hurdle Race. Unless something goes amiss with the not
very handsome animal between now and Saturday- and
always barring accidents in the race—it may be taken for
granted that Apsley will beat a large majority of the
opposition. Tim Swiveller, last year’s winner, is not
among the likely ones this time, but Bbor promises to
make a good show, and The Pioneer will find disciples if
he comes up to the scratch in sound condition. Unfor-
tunately there is no Eedleap for the unsophisticated
punter to stand or fall by, nor do the Messrs. Miller
appear dangerous at all in the chief events. Apsley may
be a “ real good thing ” for the Hurdle, yet the evidence
is somewhat doubtful, so in its fatuous anxiety to dis-
cover a “tip” The Bulletin will rather discover Bill, a
comparative outsider.
• • * *
Warwick Farm used Gray’s starting-machines
on Saturday with great success; the days of the human
article seem numbered. The A.J.C. will, no doubt, be
the last to succumb, but Randwick must bow to the
inevitable, and, much as the “ big " men dislike the idea
progress with the sporting times.
* « * *
Common and his rider, Mr. Mat Harris, were
“ sent up ” at Warwick Farm on Saturday for “ a sixer.”
The writer gave Matthew the credit of being a betttr
“ artist,” than to land himself in a mess of this sort.
• • * •
Nat Langham, the only man who beat Tom
Sayers, must have been much such a fighter as Mick
Dooley. Langham dabbed his proppy left hand into Sayers’
face until he closed both the after champion’s eyes, and
Sayers was led out of the ring sound in wind and limb, but
stone-blind. Dooley pecked at Ryan in Melbourne last week
until Ryan’s frontispiece resembled a bit of raw liver.
This is the worst feature of a “straight left” in stage
fights. The continual taps bring the gore until the fight
resolves itself into a sickening spectacle, whilst there is
nothing to show for solid and effective punches over the
heart and on “ the point.”
• * * *
A huge big crowd witnessed the Sydney Uni-
versity-Wallaroo football match on Saturday. Wallaroo
played as it never played before, beating the hitherto
invincible ’Varsity by 9 points to 5. Randwick beat
Paddington by 5 points to nil, and Pirates beat Parra-
matta by 42 points to 3.
♦ * * *
Hova has already been written by nearly every
bookmaker in Melbourne, straight out, for the Caulfield
Cup, and is the favourite pick in the cups double.
Waterbury is being quietly backed for the Grand National
Steeple, down south.
♦ * * *
Dear B., —He Waddy’s cricket, don’t forget that
it takes an Australian some time to get used to English
conditions. Waddy did do badly at first; but his last
three scores for Balliol were 35, 23, and 68. —Leg-Stump.
♦ • ♦ *
Stated in Adelaide that the results of most
senior football matches are already arranged amongst the
clubs up to Sept. Adelaide seems to easily beat Melb.
this year for “ stiff” matches.
* • * «
A certain Adelaide football club committee is
openly charged with paying a prominent player, and
efforts are vainly being made to bring the matter before
the Association. When nearly all footballers receive
remuneration, it is not likely delegates are going to cut
their own throats.
♦ ♦ • •
The popular sporting brothers who backed that
smart youth, “ Pyramids,” for Sydney Talk’s billiard
tournament two years in succession, have had hard luck.
Last year, as an unknown, he had over a century as a
handicap, and at the last minute some of the “ books,”
seeing that they were sure to lose their money, ferreted
out the fact that the young man was hardly 21, and,
therefore, ineligible to compete. This year “ Pyramids,”
without having handled a cue in public, was put back to
scratch, and despite his being drawn against Sam Grim-
wood, his party backed him heavily. But, after an
exciting tussle, experience beat brilliancy on the post.
In billiards the victory is seldom to the swift. What a
“ monte ” the previous tournament now appears to have
been for “ Pyramids ” who, with time, will make some-
thing of a champion ! The of the tourna-
ment was Mr. George Kiss.
♦ ♦ * »
Comedy is beginning to get a show in Melb.
pugilistic circles (or should it be rings ?)as well as the serious
raw-meat knock-outs which constitute the tragic side.
Before the Dooley-Ryan fight started, a spar took place
between Closson and Billy Williams, which was probably
one of the most laughable things ever seen in the way of
fighting. After hitting Williams when he was on the
ground, and punching the referee for stopping him fight-
ing after time was called, Closson earned a well-merited
victory by kicking his opponent on the easiest spot to
reach and bolting from the ring. There is a big opening
for comic prize-fighting in the near future.
Old man Grace scored an innings of 196 against
Cambridge University. It was the Cantab bowling that
provided such a picnic for the Australians last year.
• * * »
Richardson started the season well by taking
10 Derbyshire wickets for 51 runs. Lockwood, the other
fast Surrey bowler, didn’t appear among the county team
on this occasion.

Parliamentary Elections.
To the Electors of New Mouth Wales*
THE OENTEAL ELECTION COMMITTEE OP
THE PROTECTIONIST PARTY.
We earnestly ask you to consider the following
facts in connection with this election : —
Under the influence and rule of the Foreign-
trade Party we have piled up a debt to foreign
countries of .£55,000,000, for which we have to
pay two and a quarter millions interest out of our
produce.
The expenditure of these loan funds created a
boom, and directed labour into the construction of
public works, to the neglect and destruction of
reproductive industries.
The collapse of the borrowing boom, coupled
with our fiscal policy, has left us without the
necessary industries to yield employment to our
present army of workers, while we have 30,000
youths from our schools seeking admittance
annually into the industrial ranks.
To meet this position foreign-trade advocates
and journalists are advising us to put all out
energies into the production of raw material foi
export to Europe. In fact, we are advised not tc
encourage the home production of ordinary neces-
saries, so that their import may provide EM-
PLOYMENT FOR FOREIGN VESSELS tc
carry our raw produce to European markets at low
freights.
Has such a policy ever established a civilised
and prosperous community in any of the new
lands into which the surplus population of Europe
is flowing? Has it done so in any of the old
settled countries of the world ? _ On the contrary,
its failure stands out as a warning to avoid such a
policy.
Even last year we imported over and above
what we exported produce SUITABLE TO OUR
SOIL and Climate exceeding the value of
.£3,000,000, not counting iron nor manufactured
articles. To supply this home demand is our best
market, and will assist in the general development
of other and varied industries, without which we
cannot find occupations and employment for the
rising generation.
The Single-taxers have deluded the LABOUR
PARTY into the belief that a tax on land would
benefit them. They have likewise befooled the
Freetrade party that a land-tax would be a taking
cry and a good policy at the very time we are
making FREE GRANTS of land and money to
induce Village Settlement.
LAND INCOMES and ABSENTEES are all
fair subjects for taxation when the necessity for
their imposition arises, but neither time nor
circumstance warrants their imposition at the
present time.
Surely it should be evident to the meanest
capacity that a TAX ON AGRICULTURAL
LAND is equivalent to an EXCISE DUTY on
its PRODUCE, and must to that extent DETER,
SETTLEMENT.
We have all the elements required for the pro-
duction of iron and ABUNDANT LABOUR for
its manufacture, while we are IMPORTING iron
to the value of HALF A MILLION ANNU-
ALLY. Our neglect of this matter is a disgrace
to New South Wales, and shows to what an extent
we have allowed the Foreign-trade element to
dominate our councils in the face of the fact that
NO COUNTRY in the world has been able to
establish ironworks without the aid of PROTEC-,
TIVE DUTIES.
All the Colonies are on the same plane of civilisa-
tion, with equal standards of living, salaries and
wages, and therefore we have nothing to protect
us against each other. It is therefore our duty
to make a great effort to have the Colonics
FEDERATED into a GREAT AUSTRALIAN
NATION.
We earnestly ask all, and particularly young,
Australians to throw educational opinions and
PREJUDICES aside in the consideration of our
position, and put an end to this INSIDIOUS
INFLUENCE, which is being exerted by
forign IMPORTERS calling themselves AUS-
TRALIANS.
ARCHD. FORSYTH,
Chairman.

REDFERN ELECTORATE.
Mr. H. C. HOYLE,
Who is a straight-going Protectionist, is running
for the Red fern seat.
1. The Parliament will not be complete
without him.
2. —The Country requires his Services.
3. —Redfern must not lose him.
4. —He is a Consistent Democrat, a Fearless
Defender of the Working Classes, and a
Staunch Advocate of the National Cause.
As the Country is in need of men with plenty of
backbone, VOTE for HIM to a Man.

* • ♦ •
Editor Hogue, of Sydney E. News, had thoughts
of emulating Lord Rosebery by bringing off a double—
Sydney Tattersall's billiard tournament and the seat at
the Glebe. Ha has lost the first “leg,” and on the
remaining one will have a hard battle.

The Akgus, in its painful desire to be popular,
allows its football man to grog-blossom into poetry as per
sample :
“ What makes the ball love Kearney so?”
The Geelong forward cried ;
“ ’Cos Kearney loves the ball, you know,”
The barrackers replied.
The head of the man who wrote that has mistaken its
avocation. Heaven intended it for a football.
• • • *
The Sydney double-shops have once more scored
a clever victory over the authorities. After the con-
viction last week of one of their number who was pre-
scribed a month’s hard, others who had been summoned
hastily got their oases adjourned for three days. In the
interim—well, the informer, who happened to be the same
person in each information, fell ill, or something: any-
how he didn’t turn up when wanted, and so the prosecu-
tions went by the board. Meanwhile, the shops are doing
business only with known clients. Some of them have
closed up altogether, and others have washed off their
signs. The police seem to prosecute only the small shops;
the miniature Tatt.’s are left alone, and the “ohip”-
houses and sly-grog shops go on serenely as of old.
• * * »
Mr. “Ranny” Want, writes an Australian
abroad to Tub Bulletin, is a most devoted lover of the
ancient game of golf, and is fast making his way into the
front rank of players. He is one of the regular “ Sand-
wich men." The popularity of the game in England is
astounding, and a man is considered nothing if he cannot
play. Many a dead country town—notably Sandwich—
has received a new lease of life in consequence of the local
establishment of links. [Sandwich is now quite a pros-
perous place, where formerly it was the most dismal of
the buried Cinque Ports.
Parkes has been bombarded with con-
gratulations on the attainment of his 79th birth-
day last Sunday. A Sydney D.T. interviewer
went with a blithesome, springy step to dive into
the old man’s recollections of infancy, and ex-
tracted much information regarding his capacity
for goo-goo utterance and mud-pie manufac-
ture. Singularly enough, however, the veteran
omitted the one thing material, and did not say
that he owes his strength and health, under Pro-
vidence, to a judicious use of Marshall’s Tonic
Nervine, sold at corner of Pitt and Park streets,
Sydney.
What Princes can get for half-a-crown.
The Duchess of York has presented her husband
with a boy, a tiny little prince who will come to
the throne of England by and by, if all goes well.
It may take a good many years yet, but, from this
on, the young gentleman has something to live for.
However, should he never get the billet he is
promised, supposing, for instance, there were no
throne left for him to take a seat upon when his
turn comes, it may be well for him to learn as soon
as possible that a prince without a crown can
always afford to give half of one for some good
enjoyment, such as a bundle of 25 Frossard Swiss
Cigars. It is to be hoped that he will have sense
enough to look out that he gets the genuine article
for his money.
Messrs. Mcßu’rney, Wallis and Company,
wool-brokers, Sydney, following the example of
other firms, have issued their annual wool-report.
The pamphlet, to which the compiler has evidently
devoted considerable time and attention is a credi-
table production, and is replete with information
which makes it not only interesting reading, but
worthy of preservation as a work of reference.
The firm have reason to feel satisfied with their
first season’s work. Starting somewhat late,
they managed to dispose of 4,000 bales of wool,
a performance which augurs well for their
future prospects. As a proof of the rapid growth
of the popularity of the Sydney market, it appears
that during the season of 1890-1 there were sold
250,000 bales, whereas in 1893-4 the number was
increased to 400,500. A lot of useful hints are
given to squatters with regard to the preparation
of wool for the auction mart, on which each member
of the firm is fully competent to advise from a
lengthened experience acquired in the good old
school of Mort and Co. The smallest lots
sent to M‘B., W. & Co. receive as much atten-
tion as the largest, and their countinghouse is
under the able supervision of Mr. M'Dowell, who
was for 14 years with Mort and Co., and who
resigned his position to join the new firm.

Dear Bulletin,— “ says amuk
is not from Arabic, and that it’s Javanese as well
as Malay. Well, it’s also Macassar, and Bugis,
and Sassak, and a lot of other things—all modifi-
cations of Malay. The Portuguese did find the
word in Malay in 1511, as they also found “ Alla”
and “ Sheetan,” and hundreds of other words im-
ported from Arabia in the previous century, and
nearly all since modified in spelling, notwithstand-
ing the use of Arabic Ietters.— MACASSAR.
At a Northern police-court recently, a
white boy, five years old, was charged with being a
neglected child. His father sold him for ,£lO to a
Chinese brothel-keeper named Ah Kitt;_ and a
legal deed was drawn up giving Ah Kitt full
rights over the child’s person, and _ specially em-
powering him to send the boy to China. There is
a strong demand for white children in_ China for
nameless purposes, and such a traffic is so pecu-
liarly fit for a lawful Kanaka slave-centre like
Bundaberg that it seems a pity to nip it in the
bud by police-interference.
That fine old card-player, Captain David
Walker, of the A.S.N. Co., old regime, died the other
day at Enmore, Sydney. He was for many years
the co.’s senior skipper, and so strong in his posi-
tion that when, some 20 years ago, he lost the
steamer Auckland on the 90-Mile Beach, the co.’s
rule of going to the bottom of the skippers’ list
was not enforced. Some years later, however, he
lost another big steamer, and left the co, rather
than go down. The writer remembers him just 40
years ago, at 24 years of age, in command of
the Wonga Wonga, one Christmas trip, when
amongst the passengers from Melbourne to Sydney
was Sara Flower.
The other day Judge A’Beckett informed
six good Melb. men and true that they had dis-
credited the whole system of jury-trial by award-
ing £5O damages in an accident case where the
medical expenses resulting from the accident
amounted to £l5O. The jury had seemingly got
over their difference of opinion with an inane ver-
dict, as was done in the first Speight v. Syme case.
They mutually agreed to say that the father of a
child injured through the Railway Department’s
negligence was not entitled to have the entire doc-
tor’s bill paid, and they didn’t give a ghost of reason
for putting the blame upon the one side, whilst re-
fusing common justice to the other. The judge,
however, expressed himself warmly in favour of a
new trial, which must have been highly satisfac-
tory to the plaintiff and to the lawyers concerned.
A decision that brings discredit upon the Law is a
good thing for the lawyers, anyway. The absurdly
large number of legal persons who flourish in
Smellbourne is proof that the Law gets more dis-
creditable every year.
Dear Bulletin, —All your champion mean
men will take a back seat when I introduce mine.
Six years ago, when undergoing the colonial ex-
perience incidental to new chumdom, I met him
on the Murrumbidgee. He was travelling in
company with a black gin. Here are his words;
“I come of a good English county family—the
Pashes of Blankshire—but could get nothing to
do in a land where all the government billets are
filled by damned Irish. Oxford life has unfitted
me for beastly graft, as they call it. Poor Nelly, a
king’s daughter, has taken a fancy to me, and
saves me from the degradation of having to cadge
tucker from people who’d grovel before my aristo-
cratic parents,” Hereupon Nellie emerged from
a selector’s homestead and proceeded to fill his
nosebag with mutton and brownie. —Yours, V.
Colemane & Sons’ Yic. Ointment is marvellous.
Dear Bulletin, —Regarding “ the silver-
tongued orator of the United States.” Let
me tell you that the backbone of the
eloquent lecture printed in S.M. Herald of
June 9, and first delivered by 001. Bell a few days
previously, was annexed, years ago, by an
obscure American writer and speaker named
Robert G-. Ingersoll; it is printed at page 97
of a book called “Prose Poems ” (sth ed., New
York, C. P. Farrell, 1892). Ingersoll, like Bell,
commences by looking at Napoleon’s tomb, and
follows him step by step through his career—like
Bell. I have copied the peroration to show you
the unblushing nature of Ingersoll's crib :
Inobbsoll.
And I said I would rather
have been a French peasant
and worn wooden shoes. I
would rather have lived in a
hut with a vine growing
over the door, and the
grapes growing purple in
the amorous kisses of the
Autumn sun. I would
rather have been that poor
peasant, with my loving wife
by my side, knitting as the
day died out of the sky—
with my children upon my
knees and their arms
about me—l would rather
have been that man, and
gone down to the tongueless
silence of the dreamless
dust, than to have been that
imperial personification of
force and murder known as
Napoleon the Great.
Bell.
And I thought I would
rather be a peasant wearing
the burs and furz of honest
toil, with a home secure
from the fears of want or
war, where the wife could
prune her flowers in peace,
and the dimpled babe could
watch with confidence my
coming, as the shadows
lengthened on the lawn,
than to wear the name of
Conqueror, the epaulets of a
general, or a sword dangling
by my side stained by a
brother’s blood 1
The Herald says that Col. Bell’s remarks on
Lincoln were hardly less eloquent, but does not
print them. Hum! Geniuses often jump to-
gether. But can nothing be done to protect our
“silver-tongued orators” from these somewhat
brazen coincidences ?—Yours, Sir Fretful.

HERR RASSMUSSEN, PH.D.
A Few Sketches of His Interesting Life and
Work.
Herr Rassmussen, who is known throughout
the world as “ The Celebrated Danish Her-
balist,” was born in the little kingdom of Den-
mark, shortly after its last disastrous war with
Germany. His parents being wealthy, and
belonging to the most influential class, he
received a first-class education, and displayed
exceptional talent in the science of botany and
botanical chemistry, and very soon surpassed
everyone of his fellow scholars in those sub-
jects, and eventually made the science of botany
Ids life-study.
Finding his native country too small, and its
flora too limited to extend his botanical re-
searches, lie left for Australia, and landed here
in the beginning of 1879. His first object was
to learn the English language and become
familiar with the customs and habits of his
adopted country, as well as with its botanical
history and medicinal flora. Herr Rassmussen
had quite his share of the troubles and difficul-
ties which usually befall a newcomer in Aus-
tralia ; but he soon overcame every difficulty,
and after five years’ travelling through all the
colonies, investigating every known Australian
medicinal plant, and discovering many new
ones, he eventually settled down permanently
in Sydney in 1883, and then commenced a
most noble mission—that of turning his most
wonderful botanical discoveries to the benefit
and relief of suffering humanity. Herr Rass-
musseu succeeded in this gigantic undertaking
far beyond all expectations, and his name very
soon became a household word, not only in
every Australian camp, village and city, but
also throughout Europe, Africa and America.
Space will not permit to mention the
numerous cures effected by his now world-
famed Ale aline Remedies, suffice it to say
that thousands of original testimonials may be
inspected by anyone at his Head Depot, 547
George Street, Sydney, many of which may
also be seen in his celebrated work, “The
Natural Doctor.”
There is no doubt whatever that Herr Rass-
mussen deserves all the success and praise which
he has met with, because he is a most scientific,
conscientious and genuine herbalist—his last
ten years record being so wonderful that his
remedies must have cured more human suffering
in that period than is usually effected by a
hundred average doctors in a similar number of
years. He does not hold any consultation
similar to a doctor; his whole business is
purely a Proprietary Herbal Remedy Business.
He simply prepares a genuine herbal remedy
specially forthe nerves,and another for the blood,
and so on, which can always be relied upon.
He prints on the box containing the remedy its
price and all advice as to doses and diet and
other particulars, and advertises same, which
is the fairest way possible to conduct a business
of this kind. Any information or particulars
desired are given or sent absolutely free of
charge. Pamphlets are sent post free to any
person desiring one, containing all particulars
and many useful and valuable hints on health.
Herr Rassmussen has very large branches
of his extensive business in Melbourne,
Brisbane, Adelaide, Perth, Wellington (N.Z.),
and throughout the world. He is an
honorary member of several scientific socie-
ties, a gold medalist, diplomee and Hon.
Corr. Mem. of the Academic Parisienne des
Inventeurs, France ; last year he received the
Honorary University degree of Doctor of
Philosophy ; in 1887, he was presented with a
magnificent gold medal by several of his cured
patrons in Sydney, and also awarded a silver
medal in Denmark, 1888, a bronze medal,
Melbourne, 1893; and in 1891 he issued his
“ Natural Doctor,” a herbal medical work of a
high standard for which he received great
praise from the press, and which is now trans-
lated into many languages.

Editor Bulletin, — “Dromedary” asks
me, can a man run 300yds. at the same speed as
he can 100? Certainly not. There have been
several men credited with a few yards under evens
for the 100, but only one, as far as I know
(Hutchens), has ever done the 300 in even time.
The shorter the journey the greater the speed—
exactly, Mr. Dromedary. My “ phenomenon ’
was only a good riding camel, carrying a man
weighing about list. About that camel with
9001b. on his back. The man who drove the first
camels in harness in Australia told me that he
saw eight bags of flour put on a camel at The
Gums platform, S. A. The bull got up with them
all right, but they had to lift them off again with
the crane, as he would not lie down. It seems
incredible, but I think the bull camel was a pheno-
menon.— Flong. Booligal, June 10.
A monumental law-suit has just been
wound up peacefully at Creswick (Vic.). The
original cause of dispute was a drain, or an
embankment, or a culvert, or something—the
beginning of the trouble lies so far back that
it is almost forgotten—made by the Creswick
shire council, which caused an accumulation
of water on some adjacent land. The owner
asked that the damage should be made good, but
b hug met in an aggressive and infuriated spirit
he sued for compensation and was awarded .£10.
Then the Council started a series of appeals which
dragged on for many months, and cost the rate-
payers .£1400, and only ended when the Privy
Council shut down on further proceedings.
Finally, when no more litigation was possible, it
offered to fill in the offending drain, and as that
was just what the plaintiff had been asking all
along the matter was settled by two or three
pick-and-shovel men at a total cost of <£5. Mean-
while, a special rate will probably have to be
struck to cover the law costs.
A Tauranga (M.L.) girl lately made a
sensation. She had gone through a long course of
novels in which the heroine mostly “went wrong”
and was ultimately found dead in the river, with
her wealth of golden hair hanging about her face,
and a look of ineffable peace on her marble
features. At last she became possessed with a
yearning to be a heroine herself, so she wrote a
distracted letter to her mother stating that she
had been “deceived,” and had gone to end her
troubled life in the creek. Then she hid her-
self up a tree and watched while the _ police^and
the neighbours dragged for her remains. For a
while she enjoyed the situation immensely, and it
was only when the cold evening came on and she
realised that she daren’t go home to tea and bed
then she fully realised the horror of her position.
She remained up the tree for 36 hours, but when
the second evening came on she crawled down and
made for home in the dusk. She was captured by
her harried parents while trying to steal some
bread and jam out of the kitchen, and being called
to account she had to explain that she had not
been “deceived,” and was only suffering from
cramp through long residence in a tree.
Then the police were informed, and the search
parties were dismissed, and that night there were
sounds in these parts as of a heroine in great
trouble and promising with tears never to do it
again.
One gang of syndicators that M‘II wraith
met with in England wanted to construct 250
miles of Queensland railways on these terms : The
Co. to get 10,500 acres for every mile of railway
constructed, and to have first selection, so as to
get the best land and leave the worst for the
nation; the cost of building the line to be put
down at <£3500 per mile (instead of .£2500 as
estimated); and the State to pay 2 per cent,
interest on the capital invested until the line itself
pays over 4 per cent, to the shareholders. There-
fore, the situation would figure out something like
this ; Queensland can borrow money at 4 percent.,
and build her own railways, and as the lines pay
nearly 2 per cent, interest the consolidated revenue
has to find the other 2 per cent.; in other words,
Queensland can possess her own railways for a
present net loss of 2 per cent., and take all the
profits when they come, which they will in time.
The British shindykit proposes, on the other hand,
that it should own the railway; that it should get
nearly 3,000,000 acres of land—equal to about
twice the cost of the line—as a bonus for its
trouble in owning it; that the State should pay
the 2 per cent, interest just the same ; and that all
the profit—when there is a profit —should go to
the shindykit. Truly there is nothing quite so

White Feather Proprietary Gold Company f Limited
(To be registered under the Companies’ Act),
SITUATE AT WHITE FEATHER, NEAR COOLGARDIE, WEST AUSTRALIA.
Capital ----- £12,500, in 50,000 Shares of ss. each,
Of which 25,000 are issued as fully paid up to the present proprietors, and 25,000 are offered to the public on the following terms : —2s. 6d. per Share on application, 2s. Gd. per Share on allotment. Of the
amount subscribed, .£5250 will be placed to the credit of the Company, less the bare flotation expenses, estimated not to exceed .£4OO.
Pro'visional Directors :
Hon. WM. LATDLEY, M.L.C.
ALFRED HAHCOURT, ESQ.
ROBERT AMOS, ESQ.
W. E. WALMSLEY, EsQ. (Moree).
R. G. WATKINS, ESQ.
J. 15. CHRISTIAN, ESQ.
Bankers •
BANK OF NEW SOUTH WALES.
Brokers :
SYDNEY : FELIX HANDLE, Union Bank Chambers, 68J Pitt Street.
MELBOURNE: ALFRED TOLHURST, Prell’s Buildings. 58 Queen Street.
ADELAIDE : WILKINSON ANI> HARRISON, Exchange.
BALLARAT: J. D. WOOLCOTT, Exchange.
BENDIGO : D. DWYER, Beehive Exchange.
Secretary {pro. tem.): LEONARD DODDS, 131 Pitt Street, Sydney.
THIS Company is being formed to acquire from the present holders their entire interest in Gold
Mining lease No. 533 (known as M'Kenzie’s, and having an area of 15 acres), and immediately
adjoining M‘Auliffe’s Lease No. 156, which was recently purchased by Lord Percy Douglas and Mr.
W. A. Mercer for a London Syndicate. The purchase money paid for this property being .£6500 in
cash. The other adjoining blocks have been purchased at large prices by Adelaide Companies. From
advices since received great disappointment was felt by English representatives in not having acquired
this property.
As will have been seen by the reports appearing in the daily press, the White Feather portion
of the Coolgardie goldfield has been brought into great prominence of late, which has been largely
increased by the series of purchases made on behalf of English capitalists.
From the subjoined Reports by well-known mining authorities, there are two known reefs running
through the property, varying in size from Ito 2 feet, in aN. W, and S.B. course. There also appears
to be a third vertical reef, but upon which no work has been done up to the present.
The property also contains some very rich leaders, and doubtless some of these will be found to
junction in depth, giving place to another rich reef. It will be noticed that the original proprietor has
made the property pay from the start by merely dollying the stone in a primitive manner. The
adjoining properties have also been paying in a similar way.
There is an ample supply of Mining and Milling Timber in the immediate vicinity. As to water,
a most important factor in this district, there is a good and permanent supply obtainable from a lake
about three miles from the mine. Besides which, it is confidently expected that a sufficient supply will
be met with in sinking at a moderate depth.
It is estimated that the Capital to be placed to the credit of the Company, =£5250, will be more
than ample to open up the mine, erect the necessary machinery, and place it in a dividend-paying
position, when the smallness of the capital, £12,500, will tell most favourably in the results.
The Company will be declared formed when 20,000 shares have been applied for.
After allotment of the shares, a meeting of the allottees will be convened by the Provisional
Directors for the purpose of electing Directors and adopting Memorandum and Articles of Association.
The only agreement for the sale of the property is one dated 12th May, 1894, between J. R.
McKenzie and others, which can be inspected at the Company’s Office, 131 Pitt Street, Sydney.
Application for shares to be made on the accompanying form to any of the above-named brokers.
Sydney, June, 1894.
Report by W, H. Corbould, M.A.I.M.E.
May 9, 1894.
Leonard Dodds, Esq., Sydney,
Deae Sib, —I beg to submit my report on M'Kenzie’s Claim, M.L., 533. The property is situated
at the White Feather, 35 miles N.E. of Coolgardie. The property has an area of 15 acres. The lodes
being worked at present are “ Blanket lodes,” carrying very rich gold. The country is quartzite, and,
in places, the gold is showing in the quartzite. The strike of the lodes is about N.W. and S.E.,
dipping to the N.E. There appears to be a vertical lode on the property, but no work has been done
on it, so that I cannot say with certainty. Since taking up the property, M‘Kenzie has made a few
hundred pounds profit, after paying men’s wages. The two adjoining claims—Doyle’s and M ‘Auliffe’s
—have also paid all expenses and paid good dividends to the owners. The work done on M'Kenzie’s
is very primitive, and the Company will have to start and work the mine in a systematic way, which
will mean the outlay of a thousand or two pounds. Rich stone is showing in the face of the prospectors’
workings, and it may be possible to dolly enough gold to pay further prospecting of the mine at a
deeper level. In conclusion, the property is a good mining venture, worthy of being tested by a
Company.
Yours faithfully,
(Signed) W. H. CORBOULD,
Report by Robert Pettigrew,
MINE MANAGER OF THE MARITANA AND NAPIER GOLD MINING COMPANY.
Deak Sibs,— The mine that I visited a short time ago is situated at the White Feather, 35 miles
north of Coolgardie. This property is held by Mr. M'Kenzie as a mining lease, No. 533, and consists
of 15 acres. It is bounded on the S.E. by M‘Auliffe’s property, which has lately been purchased by
Mr. W. A. Mercer, of Coolgardie (of the firm of Benick, Moreing and Co., London), and it is bounded
on the S.W. by Doyle’s property (which was lately bought by an Adelaide Co,), it is hounded on
the N.W. by Meiklejohn’s property, which has been purchased by Messrs. Braidwood and Besford, of
Adelaide. All three properties are raising good stone, and have dollied out sufficient gold to pay
all working expenses for the last five months. I consider lease No. 533 is equal in value to any of the
above blocks mentioned. There are two known reefs running through this property, and a number of
rich veins. The reefs are from 12 in. to 2 feet wide.
Very little work was done on those reefs at the time of my first visit, but on my second visit a
large amount of trenching had been done, exposing the reefs. The stone taken from these trenches is
very rich in gold; this gold is well disseminated throughout the stone, and, in ray opinion, is rich
enough for dollying. Mr. M'Kenzie has been paying all working expenses by this means.
The strike of the reefs is west by north, with an underlay of 20 min. nor’-east. The hanging
wall of the reef is quartzite, the foot wall is diorite. Samples taken from the hanging wall prove this
quartzite to be gold bearing, and in Doyle’s block, 30 feet from the boundary line of McKenzie’s, I saw
large blocks of this quartzite broken from the hanging wall which was sufficiently rich for dollying.
There is a good supply of mining and milling timber on and in the vicinity of the property to last
for many years.
The water at the White Feather is obtained from a lake three miles away, where a good supply is
always obtainable, but I have no hesitation in saying that water in sufficient quantities will be got in
sinking at a depth of 160 or 200 feet.
Speaking of the White Feather generally, as a reefing field, the nature of the country leads me to
believe the gold will not be found in patches, but uniformly distributed throughout the stone. I also
think the reefs will continue to a great depth, and will increase in width and richness as depth is
attained. Developments up to the date of my last visit confirm the above opinion, and all mining
men of any standing who have visited this field are very favourably impressed with it as a reefing
field.
There is no doubt the leaders on this and the adjoining properties are immensely rich, and all
leading towards the main reefs.
Gentlemen. —l have no hesitation in recommending this property to investors as a valuable mining
property.
Yours respectfully,
Hannan’s Find, (Signed) Robert Pettigrew,
May 9th, 1894. Mining Manager Maritana and Napier Gold Mining Company.
Report hy J, Treloar,
MANAGER OF THE AGNES GOLD MINING COMPANY, HANNAN’S RUSH.
. . Hannan’s Rush, May 10th, 1894.
I have visited this property, and have great pleasure in reporting as follows :
The mine is situated about 16 miles from Hannan’s Rush, and the place is known as the White
Feather. The lease, No. 533, adjoins on the S.E. McAuliffe’s lease, which has just been purchased by
Lord Percy Douglas and Mr. Mercer for an English Company, and on the S.W., Doyle’s, which has
just been floated in Adelaide.
The property consists of 15 acres, and there has been large quantities of gold taken from the
alluvial workings on the course of those reefs. Very little work has been done on the property, as the
stone has been taken out for dollying. There are two reefs on the property, varying in width from 9 in.
to 2 feet, widening out as they go down, and can be traced right through the property.
The strike of the lode is N.W. by S.E., with an underlay to the N.E.
There are also several very rich leaders in various parts of the property, and I have no doubt that
some of those will junction at a depth, and more than probable a rich reef will be the result.
The gold is well distributed through the stone, and is a good battery stone, being free gold, and
should yield several ounces of gold to the ton of stone.
I saw the lessee taking a quantity of gold to the bank that had been dollied : it was from small
gold up to |oz. pieces. I believe this property to be one of the best situated, for most of the rich
leaders running through the properties on the White Feather. Therefore, I am quite certain the
property is a very valuable one, and I can strongly recommend it to the investing public.
I may mention that M‘Kenzie has made it pay from the start.
(Signed) J. TRELOAR,
Manager of the Agnes Gold Mining Company,
THE PROVISIONAL DIRECTORS, SYDNEY ’ J ™ E ’
WHITE FEATHER PROPRIETARY GOLD COMPANY, LIMITED,
SYDNEY.
Dear Sirs,—
„ , The following is my opinion respecting the reefs in the “ White Feather ” district,
near Coolgardie, Western Australia :
Regarding their permanence—that is assured. Timber for fuel and mining purposes, without
question, the best on the “ Yilgarn ” Goldfield, and a lasting supply of water within a few miles.
Although many of the lodes carry good dollying stone, their character is not patchy, but good
general milling quartz, the gold being both coarse and fine and equally distributed through the vein.
Body of crushing stuff varies in thickness from 2 to 4 feet, and, as a rule, increases in size with depth,
with no falling off in gold yield.
These reefs should give excellent results, having the undeniable advantages of timber and water.
I am, Yours faithfully, A. GOLDIE.

Dear Bulletin, —The champion mean man
was a Maoriland squatter. He used to always kill
his neighbour’s beef, and tell the Chinkie cook to
boil it hard so the men wouldn’t eat much —this to
save salt.—Baktrax.
Thedirectors of Rand wick Asylum, Sydney,
advertised lately for a collector—commission only.
There were exactly 163 candidates, each of whom
was interviewed personally by the directors.
Several came long distances on foot, not having
the wherewithal to ride, and yet it seemed that
the appointment was, as usual, “cut and dry.”

American millionaire Vanderbilt’s yacht
ran into a lighthouse the other day. The yacht
recovered from the shock ; but the lighthouse fell
headlong, “which illustrates,’’says London GrLOßß,
“ the difference between jerry-built and Vander-
bilt.”
Robert Merry, the never-ending Melb.
litigant, has issued another writ against the Vic.
Government for money due on account of his
ancient railway contract. =£442.40') is all that
Merry wants, and he fully expects the Supreme
Court to decide in his favour. In which case the
Treasurer would presumably add Merry to the
deficit and propose to wipe him out with a sinking-
fund.
The Bulletin’s prophecy some weeks ago
that the Queensland Government’s parsimony in
the distribution of blankets would lead to trouble
among the natives has received a tragic fulfilment.
Forty-nine aboriginals live on the Palm Islands —
19 men, 16 women, the rest children —to whom
Tozer, in the generosity of his heart, sent nineteen
blankets, with a result exhilarating in the extreme.
For some hours a free fight raged among the Palm
Islanders over the insufficient shoddy, which
eventually became the property of the nineteen
who were not hors-de-comhat
Henry Green, who, according to some of
the newspapers, suicided at Glenelg (S.A.), in
Japanese fashion, by slashing open his abdomen
with a razor, was a victim to his own—to put it
mercifully— “obstinacy.” Some years ago,
Green, physically a splendid specimen of man-
hood, was a grazier and cattle-dealer at Bourke
(N.S.W.), well-known as a sportsman, and of
considerable wealth, in fact, one of the “ Darling
Kings ” before the railway reached that river.
About eight years ago, he purchased from a
carrier giving up business, a waggon, harness, and
team of horses, the purchase-money being .£2OO,
for which he gave his cheque. The vendor of the
team disappeared for a couple of years, in fact he
admitted having been “in trouble;” he had lost
the cheque and waited upon Green to get a dupli-
cate. Green enquired at his bank and found that
the cheque had never been presented—that, so
far, he had not paid for the team. He declined,
however, to give a new cheque for the missing one
unless the carrier could give him an indemnity,
in the event of the first cheque turning up. The
carrier explained that having been in trouble it
would be impossible for him to obtain security.
Green persisted in his refusal, but the man was so
urgent for what he considered justly due to him
that Green offered .£25 to settle the matter, thus
admitting the liability. The amount was refused.
Next evening the ex-carrier went to the principal
hotel in Bourke, where Green was enjoying him-
self. He called the latter out into the passage
adjoining Cobb’s office, and repeated his request
for a settlement. Green again curtly refused.
“ Then,” said the unfortunate man, “ take that!
at the same time levelling a revolver and firing.
The ball struck Green in the back, near the spine.
As he fell, the carrier put the revolver-barrel into
his own mouth and fired, falling dead alongside
Green. The latter was paralysed from the waist
down. All available medical skill was invoked,
but the ball could not be reached. Green, as will
be noted, lingered some eight years, and then,
according to the newspaper reports, ended his
misery.
“ Why should women go blushing and
writhing, and, after all, concealing and fibbing, to
a male physician ? ’
The pair of hump-backed whales which
lately visited Hobson’s Bay seem to have been
lost solely through the squabbling of the two old
men who, being whalers of experience, were chosen
as leaders. One was a Greenlander and the other
a Hobartarian, and all their time was apparently
occupied in deprecating each other’s methods.
Alleged ad. from a Belfast (Ireland) paper :
“For sale, beautiful coloured engraving of Last
Judgment, 4ft. by 3ft., showing Protestants going
to heaven and all others hurled into the pit.” If
this yarn isn’t true it ought to be. The principal
joy of every religion consists in expecting that the
other fellow will be hurled into the pit, and the
reason why the churches hang on to their pit so
desperately is that without it there will be no
place for the other fellow to be hurled into.
Dear Bulletin, —The ’possum with a bare
patch near the base of the tail is called, in Tas-
mania, “joey-ridden.” I have never seen a buck
denuded of fur in that particular spot, but have
shot and snared many does so marked. From my
experience I believe that ’possums do not squat on
their tails either in the hollows or forks of trees,
at least not in this country. They crouch in a
forked branch like a cat, and in the hollows curl
themselves round like a sleeping fox. On one
occasion I shot a doe out of a tall gum, and when
I picked her up there was a good-sized joey on
her back also dead, and with his tail twisted
round that of the old one. —Yours, 0. H. Read,
Tas.
Dear Bulletin, —Be the hanged, burglars*
Is it not remarkable that this di cussion was as
though the “word of God” were still impregnable?
as though death and judgment, Heaven, &c., were
still identical ? But do not the highest modern
names, Huxley, Tyndal and others, give the
“ next world ” the flattest of negations? Has not
even Parkes desperately resented the lessened
support of Orangeism by certifying to “certain
and unbroken rest?” Is not suicide making
enormous strides, and are not able men recom-
mending its legalisation? How, then, does
judicial “ mercy ” to the condemned stand now ?
Whatever a man’s state, and however great his
happiness, if the grave means nothingness,
punishment has no meaning for him the instant
he actually dies. Then put against such
mortal interpretation the fifteen years of
prison, which, I believe, here represent re-
prieve, and the popular logic may be abso-
lutely topsy-turvy. The great English reviewer
drawing a model State, makes all judges, etc.,
learn by positive experience the nature of the
penalties they order. (Mr. Stead skips the slight
difficulty here met in the British legislative treat-
ment of youth). However Utopian this idea it
impresses the folly of the word “ Mercy ” as
against the gallows, used by those who know
nought of, or reflect not on, penal servitude. And
even if we swallow the awful dose of “Revela-
tion,” may not real mercy still be that of the
hangman? Dare we allot “ damnation ” to such
of these men, born and reared we know not how,
but possibly the prey of vicious circumstance from
the cradle, while such a monstrosity as his late
Lordship of Ailesbury, the millions wherewith
idiots dowered him all sunk in the basest uses,
dies of debauchery at 30- how ? Similarly
doomed? Ask any priest and get “lost” your-
self for blasphemy again t the axle-greased of
God. —James Osboene, Harden.
Alex. Riddel, an enthusiastic Single-taxer,
has got the editorship of Charters Towers Eagle,
which specified that the chosen candidate must be
a Socialist.
Dear Bulletin, —We have in our town a
sawmill owner who dispenses stores to his mill
hands. The following conversation recently took
place when a bullocky drew up a log to the mill
and said, “ Cuttin and drawin that log makes up
>£4o you owe me.” Owner : “ I can’t pay you
yet.” Bullocky : “ Well, what’ll you let me have
a fifty of flour for?’’ Owner : Eight and six.
Bullocky : “ Why, I could git it for six bob at the
otherstore if I had the money.” “Well,
you know I could do it cheaper for cash.” I think
that this beats Tyson. —Yours, &c., Aduf Ekr.

new budget taxation, if passed, will actually force
him to contribute something to the national
revenue, and he will have to economise by closing
some of his palaces. This is indeed good news.

N.S. W. Minister Slattery’s inference that,
because a prisoner has only a couple of infractions
of discipline entered against him over a period of
many years, his fitness to re-enter out-
side is assured, is fallaciously conceived. The pre-
sent writer once spoke to a gaol-governor about
the conduct of a notorious prisoner. “Good’
Of course his conduct is good. There was hardly
ever a vicious criminal whose conduct in gaol was
not exemplary.” And the deputy governor said ;
“Good? That’s just it; our worst men are all
good in gaol. It is the man with good points
about him who is fractious.”

Some lines of a poem by “ The Australian
Laureate ” (Douglas Sladen), published in his
“Selections from Australian Poetry” (Walter
Scott), It is the epic of “Mrs. Watson : A
Queensland Heroine.” This vigorous woman,
“just a mother,” with her infant, was attacked by
blacks on the desolate Queensland coast. She was
baffled and beaten back in an heroic struggle with
the marauders:—
So she stored a ship’s boiler, her tank, with provision
And water enough, as she thought, for the while,
And, taking the firearms and ammunition,
Launched out on the deep for a fostering isle.
What wonder if Gilbert, who sank in the “ Squirrel,”
Or Davis, or Baffin, or Frobisher shrank
Prom facing the strange, supernatural peril
Of crossing the sea in a water-worn tank.
Ye gods 1 She also took with her a diary in which
she kept a record of events. Everything that
could possibly come under the notice of a woman
at sea in a rusty boiler was noted down —
No drain left to drink !—yet she would not be fearful,
But in painful and feeble handwriting had writ
That her baby “ was better and more cheerful,
And condensed milk appeared to agree with it
Not even the steamer when passing so near her,

These gems are selected from an embarras de
richesses. Such are the burdens which poor Australia
has to bear at the hands of English “’Varsity”
potentates who label themselves “ Australian ” on
the strength of two or three years’ residence in
this bleeding country.
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “ Cannalin ” treatment, large quan-

throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with

About a score of pale spectators supported
wrestler Jack Graham — i.e., they mostly stood
afar olf and prayed for safety—on the occasion of
his recent experimental interview with Fitzgeralds’
lions. This long-postponed event took place
at noon, in the tent adjoining Melb. Alexandra.
Five months had elapsed since the lions last saw
a human being in their airy lair, and Graham was
the only person in Melbourne who wanted to let
them see one now. The hard-framed wrestler was
dying to go in. A possibility that he might be
dying when ho came out was suggested in every-
body’s countenance except his own, also the
incidental ceremony of heating several long irons
in a charcoal fire added a peculiar zest to the fes-
tivity. There were three lions—John Burns and
his two sisters—the same vivacious creatures
that had mauled Humphries at Albury, but grown
much bigger since then. They became unusually
blithesome when the lock of the cage door was
tested. Burns, in particular, danced for joy with
such abandon that the spectators withdrew their
moral support to a greater distance off, and com-
pared notes as to their respective plans in the
event of the den falling asunder. Dan Fitzgerald
having provided himself and two menagerie atten-
dants with red-hot irons, the new tamer went in
on the nod. He didn’t pause to knock at the
gate, or scratch his head while entering. He just
got there as soon as po-sible, and waved a greeting
to the lions with his little crowbar. Fortunately
they didn’t rush to meet him, and on the
whole they behaved fairly well. When Graham
moved, the 1 ions moved. Once John Burns showed a
disposition to slap the visitor on the back, but Dan
Fitzgerald happened to intervene with his hot iron,
and the pleasantry was never perpetrated. After
the more peaceful of the lionesses had jumped
over a board in a manner strangely docile by com-
parison with the other two beasts, who simply
reared upon their hind legs, or stood on their tails,
and clamoured for gore, it was unanimously
decided that Jack Graham had done his duty, and
the curtain fell amidst loud applause. He had nob
tamed the lions much, but the lions, on the other
hand, had not tamed him. T T nder these circum-
stances the animals were engaged to give their
performance in public for the future, and the com-
pany went off to reconstruct their shattered nerves
with a drink. The dauntless wrestler was the
only man in the crowd who didn’t need a tonic.

Dear Bulletin,— Your “Wild Gat” man ad-
vises Fire Insurance Co.’s to stand oS from flat-footed
men and “persons who turn the palms of their hands
backward when walking," if they would be saved from
destruction. Now, 1 never before heard that these pecu-
liarities were included in Nature’s signs of a wrong-’un ;
yet no sooner had 1 read that par. than a vision of a
certain “ gent one,” now doing his five years, arose before
me. He was the ugliest walker I ever saw—splay-footed,
and with a habit of using his arms like a pair of crutches,
the palms turned backward and the fingers spread all
abroad. On his passage from England some 12 years ago
he was regarded as the butt of the second saloon, and was
commonly known as “ Spoopendyke," or “ Spoopy,” for
short. He could never walk a dozen paces without grab-
bing hold of a rope and holding on as though the ship
were going from under him. In that position, with his
foolish-looking mouth wide open, he appeared the mildest-
looking solicitor who ever pocketed a trust-account. This
personage changed his name after he landed, and thereby
hangs a tale—but 1 will postpone that tale.—Yours,
A. W. J.

Although the N.S.W. Government professes
anxiety to stop the rabbit plague, it has absolutely
declined to give a trial to a specific said to have been
found infallible in Maoriland. The Minister of Lands adds
that the oiler of £25.000 has been withdrawn. The pre-
paration was offered to N.S.W. free for trial in any quan-
tity on condition that if it did not absolutely clear any
run of rabbits in a month the National Wool Co. would
forfeit £5OO, but if it answered, the Co. would want the
£25,000. The offer was refused.

A big pastoral Co., in Melbourne, was inter-
viewed last week by a particularly frowsy individual, who
wanted to borrow on a “stock mortgage.” As rich men
“run in all colours,” the manager, regarding him as an
“ eccentric ” only, invited him into the office to glean
particulars as to where the stock was depasturing, how
many head, &o. But when the would-be borrower had to
start undressing himself “to round ’em up,” as he
placidly remarked, the manager promptly closed negotia-
tion] and the deal did not come off.

Thanks to Victoria’s excessively high spirit
duties, it is absolutely unsafe to order whisky in most
Melb. suburban houses. No matter what brand you name,
you will, twenty to one, get a blend of illicit spirit, and
an ulcerated throat to follow. In most of the side-street
pubs, you can see the thick oil at the bottom of the bottle.

told the writer that he attributed his gains, both in
mining and racing, not to luck, but to his being “ Able to
keep a secret." He can’t deny, however, that it was just

tralia fell due (and was promptly withdrawn) four days
before that Institution “ shut up shop.” By the way, one
of Martin’s original partners in the rich claim at Ballarat
is living with scarce a copper in Ireland whilst the other
(who took £lO,OOO for hU share) has a small Irish farm
and just makes a comfortable living. £200,000 would
not buy Martin up.

Dear Bulletin,—The hardship in connection
with the liability of mortgagors, pointed out by a corres-
pondent in your last issue, applies with greater force to
lessees—that is, people who take a lease in the first in-
stance and assign it to someone else. An original lessee is
always liable on the covenants contained in his lease,
notwithstanding the fact that it gives him power to
assign, and that he has assigned. For instance, if a man
takes a lease for, say, 15 years and assigns it to another,
and that person fails, 10 years after, to comply with its
terms, the landlord can compel the original lessee to pay
the rent for the unexpired term, and comply with the
other covenants in the lease. I don’t think this view of
the law, which is unfortunately correct, is generally
known. 1 know that not long since I raised the question
in connection with a lease, and a leading firm of solicitors
in Sydney were very much surprised when they found
that their client was liable, after they had advised him
that he was free. When a lessee assigns a lease, with the
concurrence of his landlord, he should get a release from
the covenants of the lease.— Paolo Macari.

Dear Bulletin, —The Q. Sugar-lands Guarantee
Act, which enables the Govt. to advance money to cane-
farmers on the security of their land, has so far been
hardly taken advantage of except to boom land values ;
and 1 will tell you why. All the best land in the province
is mortgaged to the banka. The banks have any amount
of money idle, and if they really wished to help the
farmers, it is the easiest thing in the world for them to
advance the money to build the mills themselves. They
are much better able than the Govt, to watch over the
expenditure and keep straggling debtors up to scratch.
But no! they put up the Govt, as a stalking-horse,
to take all the risk, while they collar the profits.
The mortgaged land is worthless now ; when the mill
is up, it will have a value at once. The hitch at present
is that the banks refuse to give the Govt, a first mortgage
on the land, and all the managers in all the little country
towns are pressing the farmers to clamour and shriek
until the Govt consents to take a second mortgage.
Then the doubly-mortgaged serfs have still to borrow
more from the banks to put in a crop of cane. Once
the mill is up, a little pressure from the banks,
and they will explode. The straw shareholders in-
solvent, the Govt, must sell the mills, which will be
bought by the banks, who will drive the few good
shareholders insolvent too. Then the banks will
have land and mills, and the way will be clear for
a big boom on the English market, an incursion of aristo-
cratic white planters working with New Guinea labour,
and the realisation of Miss Floral Shaw’s beautiful dream.
That will put the banks straight again. Some of them
are mighty crooked now, after having advanced millions
of depositors’ money to their political friends—who, I
must say, do their best to repay the obligation. Your*,
Meg ass.

Mr. L. W. Marsland, of Charters Towers, sums
up freeholder's status under Q. mining law in a lucid
pamphlet. That law is briefly this: There are two in-
cidents in legal property—property and possession, it a
wears his own watch he has both property and possession
in the watch. If A lends the ivatch to B, A retains pro-
perty, and B has possession. If A gives the watch to B,
both property and possession vest in B. Q. Supreme
Court decides that a Crown freehold grant does not carry
gold and silver beneath the surface —they remain the pro-
perty of the Crown. Two judges (Griffith and Real) hold
that possession passes to the freeholder; one (Harding)
holds that both property and possession are retained by
the Crown. An appeal is to be made to the Privy Council.
The point lies here. A cannot lend his watch
to both B and Oat the same time. But the Supreme
Court decides that he has lent it to B, and O cannot have
it. Thus B can act, as at Charters Tow’ers, the part of the
dog in the manger, refusing to use that which he does not
use himself. There are freeholds at Charters Towers, the
minerals under which can only be profitably worked from
existing deep shafts. To sink new shafts on the freeholds
themselves would not pay. But, relying on the majority
decision of Judges Griffith and Real, the freeholders can
refuse ingress to work the minerals 1000 feet below the
surface —though these minerals are not their property
unless they are paid to consent. If Judge Harding is
correct, the freeholder has no rights beneath the surface,
and the minerals can be leased and worked by the first
applicant as if the surface were held as Crown lands.
Whatever the Privy Council may decide to be
the law, the principles of equity and of general goldfields
legislation in Australia demand that the freeholder should
either work the minerals under his land or allow others
to work them without fee. As the holder of the surface,
he might be conceded a preferent right to the minerals on
fulfilling labour conditions; failing this, the first applicant
should be empowered to wmrk them. No compensation
whatever should be paid unless surface interests were
damaged. Of course it is a mistake to alienate freehold
on goldfields at all; but on the Towers the mischief was
done ignorantly. Some years ago Horace Tozer, now
Q. Minister for Mines, proposed a surface-hold tenure, by
which the freehold should be carried down to 50ft., say,
from the surface, and all beneath should be Crown lands
available for goldfield lease. The scheme had a good deal
to recommend it, but came to nothing, the Gy tuple
miners, to whom it was tentatively suomitted, preferring
the present leasehold tenures held by use and residence.
On many grounds it may be held that their judgment was
sound. Land speculation, with its concomitants of
gambling, booming, and bankruptcy, is almost wholly
stopped, and the loss of overdrafts and palatial buildings
can be borne with. So successful indeed has leasehold
tenure been in lessening the train of rent and interest
victims on goldfields that it could be applied to other
township residence areas without the slightest difficulty.

Examinations in insolvency supply Melbourne
with some of its ghastliest specimens of legal robbery
these bankrupt times. Nothing practical seems to come
of the average insolvency examination, except costs.
There are Strange Disclosures and Humorous Proceed-
ings, and occasionally “a witness” is Rebuked by the
Judge; but one never hears of unhappy creditors pro-
fiting by the assignee's action. The examination is seldom
more or less than a job for a barrister who assumes an air
of meaning to “ get to the bottom ” of the case for the
sake of people other than himself. The insolvent is
examined, and adjourned, and examined some more,
and then the inquiry closes in a hurry. It is cut short
in its prime and buried. The lawyers hive got
to the bottom of the funds provided by the trustee, and
the trustee presently informs any creditor who applies
for a dividend that there would have been a dividend if
the examination (which he usually orders without hem;
authorised) hadn’t eaten up the balance of the ass ts. A
creditor requires time for getting at this horrible fact,
the caretaker of this vanished estate beimr invariably out
when he calls for information, whilst the clerk never
knows whether the affair will “ show any thing ’ or not.
If Baron Rothschild were to file his schedule for fun, the
trustee’s clerk would have doubts as to ibe < stale
“showing anything.” Also, it would be a long time
before a red cent was shown, anyway.
* * * ♦
There is indisputable evidence that gold-stealing
goes on very largely on the Queensland gold-fields. A
return furnished to the Warden by the banks enables him
to distinguish purchases of bullion from those of alluvial
gold, and also those from places outside the particular
goldfield in which the institution carries on business.
Gold can be thus assigned to its locality. Bank purchases
of bullion nearly always exceed the returns of mills and
other reductive works. On certain goldfields the amount
of gold not traceable to legitimate sources amounts to
thousands of ounces annually. The Under-secretary for
Mines draws attention to a measure submitted to the
Volksraad by the Witwatersrand Chamber of Mines, to
license buyers of gold, who will be compelled to make a
statutory declaration of the circumstances under which
the gold was obtained. As any intelligent gold-stealer
will cheerfully make ten statutory declarations if neces-
sary, this system would probably be found interesting, if
not useful.
• * • •
When State or municipal loans are issued in
France it is a case of first come first served; the sub-
scribers roll up at the counter and dump down their
money, and if the loan is in demand the late comers
are disappointed. When the city of Paris issued
its recent loan a crowd waited all night with its money
outside the doors of the Credit Lyonnais, to rush the
place as soon as it opened in the morning. Among them
was a young clerk whose employer had sent him along
with a big roll of bank notes. All night he gripped the
precious bag of money with a firm conviction that it
would be stolen, and with an awful sense of responsibility
upon him. It wasn’t stolen, but the sense of responsi-
bility was too heavy, and he went off his head in the night,
and in the morning a lunatic was found at the front door
of the Credit Lyonnais gibbering feebly and grabbing a
bag of money with both hands.

The dodges of even eminent firms in the furni-
ture-trade. A suite of Chinese-made furniture is sent to
a white furniture-maker, who is ordered to build one suite
exactly duplicating the Celestial production. This having
been done, the Chinese cabinet-makers are kept busily
going with orders for similar suites. A purchaser comes
along and is shown the Caucasian-made set, and buys.
But he doesn’t get that one. He receives a Chow-made
suite, while the “ decoy ” suite is set out in another part
of the show-room, to await the coming of the next Chow-
hating fly, who wants furniture made by white labour.
Since the Chinese work at starvation rates, about 16 hours
a day, and all day Sunday, the extra margin of profit
made by the artful furniture-sellers may be guessed at
rather than told.
